Hugging Face CEO Clément Delangue on what Nvidia's open-source power grab really means — 3/19/26

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·29:40 ·4.5K views · 2026-03-19
Clément Delangue (CEO, Hugging Face) Jensen Huang (CEO, NVIDIA)
> "Nvidia recognized that they need everyone to be able to build AI, not just a few players.
TL;DR: Nvidia is strategically pivoting from hardware-centric "lock-in" to an open-source platform model with NemoClaw to secure long-term dominance in the AI inference era and counter the growing global influence of Chinese open-source models. The Shift to Inference: While...

TL;DR: Nvidia is strategically pivoting from hardware-centric "lock-in" to an open-source platform model with NemoClaw to secure long-term dominance in the AI inference era and counter the growing global influence of Chinese open-source models.

Nvidia’s "Platform Moat" Strategy

  • The Shift to Inference: While Nvidia dominated the "training" era via CUDA lock-in, the "inference" era (running models) is more competitive as hyperscalers build custom silicon.
  • NemoClaw as an Operating System: Nvidia’s launch of NemoClaw—a free, open-source platform for AI agents—is a strategic move to become the "Android of AI."
      • Commoditization: By providing the software layer for free, Nvidia makes it harder for labs like OpenAI or Anthropic to squeeze them on pricing or bypass their hardware.
      • Monetization via Infrastructure: Like Google (Android/Search) or Microsoft (IE/Windows), Nvidia gives away the software to drive demand for the underlying "token factories" (GPUs and data centers).
  • Aligned Incentives: Hugging Face CEO Clément Delangue notes that Nvidia’s success now depends on everyone being able to build AI, not just a few "closed-garden" labs.

The Geopolitical Race for Open-Source Dominance

  • The Rise of China: For the first time, downloads of open-source models from Chinese providers (e.g., DeepSeek, Qwen) have surpassed American providers, even within the U.S.
  • Sovereign AI: Delangue argues that the U.S. needs an "American King of Open Source" to ensure foundation models reflect democratic values and entrepreneurship rather than the biases of foreign competitors.
  • The New American Champion: Nvidia is currently dethroning Meta as the primary American contributor to open source, releasing new models or datasets almost daily.

Enterprise Adoption and the Hybrid Model

  • Fortune 500 Integration: Approximately 30% of Fortune 500 companies currently use Hugging Face for open-source models; Delangue predicts this will become a majority by 2027.
  • The 90/10 Split: Future enterprise workflows are expected to be a mix:
      • Proprietary APIs: Used for the "frontier" (top 5% of complex tasks) where labs like OpenAI still hold a performance edge.
      • Open Source: Used for 90% of general workflows to reduce costs, increase speed, and maintain data security.
  • The Skill Gap Barrier: Historically, open source required specialized ML engineers. This barrier is collapsing as AI agents allow standard software engineers—and even non-technical users—to fine-tune and deploy models.

The Evolution of AI Agents

  • Agents as Users: By the end of 2026, Hugging Face expects to have more "agent" users (autonomous programs) on its platform than human users.
  • Software as a Service for Agents: Platforms are shifting to accommodate a "Token Factory" economy where agents are the primary customers and decision-makers for which models to run.
  • Reduced Concentration of Power: Unlike proprietary APIs, open-source software is easily "forked" (copied and modified), preventing any single company from maintaining a total monopoly on how AI agents function.

Notable Quotes

"Nvidia recognized that they need everyone to be able to build AI, not just a few players. And so that’s why they’re supporting open-source AI so much... they became, in my opinion, the American king on open-source AI." — Clément Delangue
"If you have only one country, for example, only China that is dominating all open source, then it creates a bunch of risks... it creates a bunch of biases... you want basically every single country in the world to be able to train their own foundation models." — Clément Delangue
"Jensen’s product isn’t NemoClaw, it’s the compute and GPUs underneath it... He's making sure that no single customer ever gets powerful enough to walk away or even just get the upper hand." — Deirdre Bosa
"By the end of the year we're going to have more agent users on Hugging Face than human users... because everyone can use agents now to fine-tune and train models." — Clément Delangue
Hey everyone, it’s Thursday, March 19th. Welcome to another CNBC live stream. I’m Deirdre Bosa. Well, GTC just wrapped and everyone is talking about new chips, Blackwell integration, and the usual GPU horsepower. But I think the most important thing Jensen Huang did this week actually had nothing to do with silicon. He launched NemoClaw, jumping on that open claw frenzy bandwagon. Its software ecosystem made it nearly impossible to switch to a competitor. That’s CUDA. NemoClaw is free and it runs on competitors' chips too. That sounds like a crazy reversal for a company that became dominant thanks to that lock-in until you think about past technology shifts. Google gave away Android because the product wasn’t actually the operating system; it was search. Same idea here. Jensen’s product isn’t NemoClaw; it’s the compute and GPUs underneath it. The rise of deepfakes and how to stop them | FT Film

Financial Times(1.4M) ·23:33 ·15.1K views · 2026-03-19
Melissa Heikkilä (AI Correspondent, Financial Times) Vijay Balasubramaniyan (CEO, Pindrop) Henry Ajder (Deepfake AI Expert)
Deepfake technology has evolved from a complex academic pursuit into a low-cost, highly accessible tool for sophisticated fraud and misinformation, shifting the burden of verification from the individual to a nascent infrastructure of digital watermarking and global regulation.
TL;DR Deepfake technology has evolved from a complex academic pursuit into a low-cost, highly accessible tool for sophisticated fraud and misinformation, shifting the burden of verification from the individual to a nascent infrastructure of digital watermarking and global...

TL;DR

Deepfake technology has evolved from a complex academic pursuit into a low-cost, highly accessible tool for sophisticated fraud and misinformation, shifting the burden of verification from the individual to a nascent infrastructure of digital watermarking and global regulation.

The Democratization of Synthetic Media

  • Low Barrier to Entry: Creating high-quality deepfakes no longer requires technical expertise; free software and basic smartphone apps can now clone faces and voices with minimal data.
      • FT AI Correspondent Melissa Heikkilä demonstrated that a convincing face-swap and voice-clone (using tools like ElevenLabs) can be produced in minutes on a standard laptop.
  • Economic Impact of Scams: Criminals are moving beyond simple phishing to "spectacularly effective" visual impersonation.
      • Case Study: A deepfake of FT’s Martin Wolf was used in an Instagram investment scam viewed by millions.
      • Historical Precedent: In 2015, fraudster Gilbert Chikli used a physical rubber mask to impersonate the French Defense Minister on low-quality Skype calls, stealing $85 million.
  • Physical vs. Digital Realism: While AI is rapid, physical "hyper-realistic" silicone masks remain the gold standard for high-end film production.
      • Raoul Peltier (CEO, Anthrotek) notes that AI face-swaps still struggle with natural movement and lighting, whereas silicone replicas behave exactly like human skin.

The Detection Landscape: A Cat-and-Mouse Game

  • Technical Anomalies: Detection services like Pindrop analyze audio and video for "spatial and temporal" inconsistencies that the human eye misses.
      • Human Fallibility: In tests, humans often focus on the wrong cues—such as awkward hand movements—while failing to notice a completely synthetic face.
  • The Cost Asymmetry Advantage: There is a significant economic advantage for defenders in the AI arms race.
      • Detection vs. Generation: Pindrop CEO Vijay Balasubramaniyan notes it is four orders of magnitude (10,000x) cheaper to detect a deepfake than it is to generate a perfect one.
      • To succeed, a deepfake must be perfect in every dimension (accent, movement, demographics), whereas a detector only needs to find a single mistake.
  • Obsolescence of "Telltale Signs": Expert Henry Ajder warns against teaching the public specific "telltale signs" (like lack of blinking or extra fingers).
      • Developers quickly "train out" these flaws once they are identified, rendering public advice redundant and unfairly placing the burden on the average user to become a "digital Sherlock Holmes."

Verification Standards and Global Regulation

  • Technical Solutions: Industry leaders are moving toward "Content Provenance" rather than just detection.
      • C2PA and Watermarking: The Coalition for Content Provenance and Authenticity (C2PA) aims to bake "metadata signals" into content at the point of capture (e.g., inside the camera) to track its history across the web.
      • Labeling Challenges: Platforms like Meta face backlash when labeling edited photos as "Made with AI," as creators feel the labels lack nuance between minor touch-ups and total synthesis.
  • Legislative Trends: Lawmakers are shifting from voluntary norms to mandated transparency.
      • United States: The proposed "NO FAKES Act" seeks to give individuals federal control over their own likeness and voice.
      • European Union: The EU AI Act (specifically Article 50) aims to mandate transparency, potentially framing the right to know what is "real" as a fundamental human right.
  • The "Resignation" Risk: Experts warn against the "aura of inevitability" regarding AI-generated fakery.
      • Claire Leibowicz (Partnership on AI) argues that the industry must reject the idea that truth is "lost," urging journalists and tech companies to push for pragmatism over resignation.

Notable Quotes

"In order to create a deepfake, it has to take care of everything about you... In order to detect a deepfake, all we need to do is catch one mistake."

— Vijay Balasubramaniyan, CEO of Pindrop

"By saying to the everyday person, 'Here’s what you can do to spot a deepfake,' it places the responsibility, which is unreasonable, on the everyday person to become a kind of digital Sherlock Holmes."

— Henry Ajder, Deepfake AI Expert

"There’s this aura of inevitability in technology development that I think we need to push back on... I want to make sure that real people feel they can have a say."

— Claire Leibowicz, Partnership on AI

New AI technologies have made deepfakes—digitally altered images and videos of someone's face—easier than ever to create. Anyone with a smartphone can steal your image and reuse it. It really takes no technical skill; it takes an app store, and it takes a keyboard. In the age of deepfakes, no one is safe. Bad actors can use freely available AI tools such as Grok to transform images of people against their will and without their consent. This technology has also been embraced by criminals who have been able to use deepfakes to scam companies and individuals out of millions of pounds. If you could see the person, and the person sounds exactly like someone you know, it's going to be spectacularly effective. Thanks to these AI tools, it's becoming almost impossible to verify what's real and what's not. We're looking at the rise of deepfakes: where they came from, why they matter, and what we can do about them. And the best way to do that is to figure out how to make one myself. We're going to make a deepfake of us. It might be creepy; I think it probably will be. But I'll show you how easy it is. All I have to do is drag this video we found in our archives of you into the platform, and then my headshot. And so this is completely free software; it can run locally on your laptop. Okay, so processing. Oh my god. I know. I know. Okay, now that's done. Ready? Yeah. This is so eerie already. So this year as we approach busy season, KPMG's junior auditors won't be able to use their recharge system anymore. They won't be able to claim back for any hours they work over their 50 contracted hours. This means that they'll lose about 1,000 to 2,000 pounds that they were able to claim back before, roughly. I feel like this is what it would look like if we had a baby. Looks pretty good. I sound Great British. I feel like if I saw this on social media, oh I don't know, I don't know whether I would be able to tell that it was AI. I think that I would, but maybe that's because I know that it's AI. I looked like you, or really... I looked like you. Exactly. But it still had your voice. But if we wanted to change that, so put my voice on top of yours, that would be really easy as well using ElevenLabs' technology. I just drag your audio and generate speech. So this year as we approach busy season, KPMG's junior auditors won't be able to use their recharge system anymore. They won't be able to claim back for any hours they work over their 50 contracted hours, which means that they'll lose about 1,000 to 2,000 pounds that they were able to claim back before, roughly. This still has a British accent, so it clearly can't convert that. My voice is a lot less nasal in that than it usually is. So our producer has combined the two deepfake clips into one video, and we sent it to this company called Pindrop to see if they could figure out what we did. Do you want to hear what they said? Yes. Okay. If you look at the audio-video that you folks created, they're really, really good. In fact, you know, when we asked some humans to look at it and say "What do you see that's weird here?", the thing that they pointed to is the hand movement. And that hand movement feels like that was exactly the hand movement that the other reporter actually used. They moved their hands uncomfortably, so the humans picked up the completely wrong thing on those deepfakes when the AI engines actually picked it up. They picked up on their hands but not the face. Yeah. And these were also humans that were primed to ask the question "Is this AI or is this real?", so they were already looking out for any issues, and even then they didn't necessarily spot that the faces looked uncanny. In the age of deepfakes, no one is safe. The FT's Martin Wolf experienced this first-hand when his likeness was used by scammers to create a deepfake of him to sell investment advice on social media. I received a WhatsApp message, I think, from a former colleague, someone I knew fairly well, to tell me that he'd seen me on an advertisement on Instagram. If you don't want to miss it, join us now. It was clearly an attempt to represent me talking, although the accents weren't perfect, it was pretty good, and the appearance was pretty plausible. And it was selling or recommending investments. I predicted Amazon would be the biggest winner soon after it surged 89 percent. I'm Martin Wolf. I was surprised and shocked at first. It seemed so weird, simply to see this. But afterwards, when I realized the scale of the scam, basically a million people had viewed this in the EU alone, so if you include the UK and the US it could easily have been two or three million. It was impossible not to imagine that quite a few people had clicked on it and, quite probably, they'd lost a lot of money. And that's very, very upsetting. But in order to protect ourselves from these scams, we need to know what we're dealing with. So how can we detect deepfakes? We called Pindrop, a company trying to combat digital fakery. They specialize in detecting deepfakes in video and audio calls. We do that by answering two questions: is this a human? And then the second thing we answer is: is this the right human? We have partnerships with Zoom, Cisco, Microsoft Teams, and soon Google, such that we're able to provide these capabilities in each of these video conferencing systems. We're looking for minor anomalies in both audio and video to determine if it's a deepfake. So to give you an example, when we're doing this video call, there are 16,000 samples of my voice every single second. And what we're looking for is: is there an issue with that voice every second and as it progresses over time? So those are both spatial and temporal characteristics, and we're doing that both on the audio and the video side. So the better quality you have of audio and video, the higher quality deepfake you can create. But, you know, with GenAI, what's also happened is GenAI has contributed rapidly to denoising capabilities in both audio and video. So even if you have fairly weak audio, these GenAI systems are able to clean up that audio or video and then make really good representations of you. And so can you do it with just like a regular laptop or even a smartphone? You could create an audio deepfake with a smartphone; a video deepfake you'll probably need a laptop with a beefy enough processing unit because, especially if you're trying to do this in real time, that's when things get a little messy. If you don't have the real-time constraint, you can absolutely create a video deepfake on a smartphone itself. One of the things that gives me great hope is the fact that in order to generate a deepfake and in order to detect a deepfake, there is a cost asymmetry. It's four orders of magnitude cheaper to detect it than generate it. In order to create a deepfake, it has to take care of everything about you: how you speak, how you move, your accent, your demographics, all of that. In order to detect a deepfake, all we need to do is catch one mistake. These deepfake engines are going to get better, but the deepfake detection engines are going to keep par because it's so much cheaper to detect a deepfake. The first face-swapping algorithms came out in the late 90s. But even by the time this paper came out in 2008, it was still difficult, time-consuming, and expensive to achieve. Which is why you could argue that the first face-swapping scams were not digital at all. If you wanted to scam someone out of a lot of cash in 2015, your best bet was a Skype call and a fake face made out of rubber. Which is exactly what Gilbert Chikli did in 2015. So by putting a desk, a telephone, some documents, and a couple of flags behind him, he was able to pull off the most remarkable scam. The ruse worked surprisingly well until it didn't, and Chikli was caught. The FT's Victor Mallet was there for the trial. The rubber mask scammer in France was this guy called Gilbert Chikli, who was like a Franco-Israeli crook essentially. And he and an accomplice pretended to be Jean-Yves Le Drian, the then Defense Minister of France. Most of the calls were video calls on Skype from an alleged secret location below the Defense Minister, you know, in the basement as it were. The screen was slightly fuzzy, so you couldn't see his features that well, so the rubber mask obviously was sufficiently convincing in those circumstances of that rather poor quality Skype call for it all to happen. And they extracted something like 85 million dollars from the people who fell for this. Basically, he was asking for money pretending to be, you know, on a secret mission from the French state to get money to pay ransoms for hostages. And of course they were told to keep absolute secrecy about this and to transfer the money to a secret account, which they did. There were also lots of recordings of Chikli when he was in jail boasting about it as being the scam of the century, which you know, in some ways it was. The known victims were the Aga Khan, who was the head of the Ismaili Muslims of the world at the time. I think he gave something like 20 million euros to different accounts in places like China and Poland and so on. And then there was the owner of the Chateau Margaux vineyard in France, Corinne Mentzelopoulos, who gave something like 5 million or more, and then there was also a Turkish businessman who sort of confessed up and said that he had been scammed as well. And the interesting thing is that we don't know whether there might have been more people who, of course, didn't come forward and admit that they had been scammed. So in the course of that trial, it emerged that there were other people whose rubber masks could be used to deceive potential victims of this. I think they actually found a rubber mask of Prince Albert, but they also suspected that they were going to try to impersonate President Emmanuel Macron. And in a way, what it shows is that low-tech solutions can still be very effective. Would I have fallen for it? I don't think so, but then I would never have been a mark because I don't have enough money to hand over to free a hostage. So how do you make a mask that is so good it could fool a stranger? To answer that question, we spoke to Anthrotek, a company making high-quality masks for the film industry. I am Raoul Peltier, I am CEO of Anthrotek. I used to be a scientist for most of my life, and on the side of being a scientist, I used to have a hobby where I was making monster masks in my garage. Eventually started to pick up, I started to be contacted by artists—musical artists at the start a lot—and then eventually a little bit by the cinema industry. This pushed me to maybe expand a little bit the business from the spare room to a bigger garage. And eventually I took a leap of faith, decided to do mask full-time, and that's when I met my co-founder Nasmus, who convinced me that having a science background and a mask-making hobby could be all fused together. We formed a company that does hyper-realistic simulation for the medical and the robotic industry, which is where we are now. We make synthetic versions of the human body. So we make synthetic replicas of skin that behaves like skin; we make synthetic organs that behave and maybe have the right blood pressure and microfluidics, similarly to the human body. Or simply, sometimes we just make realistic faces that look like a real human. So it was a quite elaborate scam. Also 10 years ago it probably would have been more difficult than it is now to get your hands on a hyper-realistic mask. One thing that isn't known is where he got the mask from, whether he did it himself—this is quite unlikely—or whether he managed to source it from a company that maybe doesn't have such an ethical attitude as we do. I promise you I was never contacted to try and impersonate the Minister of Defense. The story was crazy and I certainly remember my friends all forwarding me the article. Yes, so this is a hyper-realistic face that was created by award-winning special effect artist David Malinowski and was painted by Anna, one of our artists. And that just exemplifies how realistic a face you can make with years of training. So this is a silicone slab; so this particular face is a training face, so they are used by special effect artists to practice painting. So the combination of various technologies—using hyper-realistic masks and AI to do fake voices—that certainly has a very strong potential for enhanced scamming ability. And that's something as a business we take very seriously. This is why, you know, we don't really make masks of someone if we're not able to physically have them in the building to check their identity. With the AI ability to do deepfake videos and face swap, there is potentially less need for hyper-realistic masks and people are going to switch to a more virtual-based way to scam people. But to be honest, we still have the same amount of work just because the technology isn't there yet. When you do a face swap using AI, it does not move naturally. And for that reason, for any sort of stunt or face replacement, most movie production, if they can afford it, will rely on hyper-realistic masks. It's just that, you know, the motion, the way the light interacts with the mask is just a lot more natural; it's very hard to replicate lighting effects onto a mask. Most cinema production will still rely on hyper-realistic masks and I don't think this will change too soon. To understand what the deepfake landscape of today looks like, I spoke to Henry Ajder, an expert in synthetic media. The landscape when it comes to detecting deepfakes has always been adversarial—that is, it's kind of a cat-and-mouse game. You know, models are released, they have certain flaws, those flaws are identified, they're often kind of broadcasted as kind of, you know, "this is what to look for," and then the models change and often those flaws are trained out. What this means is over the last eight years there's been lots of different flaws that have been kind of the telltale sign of how to spot a deepfake, whether that was, you know, not blinking—that was some of the very early signs—all the way through to a six fingers or text in backgrounds being kind of jumbled nonsense. The problem is it's evolving so quickly, and people are fine-tuning to train these flaws out, that by the time an article or a piece of information is shared about "Here's the top five things to look for," it's almost always redundant. And in some respects, I think it actually places the emphasis in the wrong place. It's a little bit like talking about how you can reduce your carbon footprint. By saying to the everyday person, "Here's what you can do to spot a deepfake," it places the responsibility—which is unreasonable—on the everyday person to become a kind of digital Sherlock Holmes. Actually, we're talking about a much bigger problem when it comes to the way that our digital infrastructure now functions in a world where anything could be fake because everything can now be generated using AI to a hyper-realistic level. And when a deepfake generator creates a deepfake, what is actually happening? Could you walk us through the technology and what's happening under the hood? There are so many different kinds of AI-generated content out there now. To say kind of, how do you make a deepfake—you know, we've got kind of diffusion-based models for creating images that learn how to basically take noise and remove noise to create images based on a prompt. We have specific lip-synchronization tools that learn how to replace very particular elements of the mouth. You know, we have the voice cloning which works on a different level, and then we also have even things like creating full-body avatars, right? So there are loads of different techniques. It's not like there's one GenAI algorithm to rule them all, so to speak, when it comes to creating deepfakes. There's a whole range of different cases, but for face swapping, auto-encoders still kind of rule the roost, so to speak. For myself, I've got quite a lot of footage of me online; if someone wants to try and clone my face or my voice, this documentary is going to be a great sample for them to be able to create this content. And because it now only takes a couple of images to create something which is good enough, it's not feasible to say to people, "Completely banish yourself and your online presence." So it's really hard, particularly for young women with deepfake image abuse, which is still the biggest challenge that we really face in terms of number of victims. A lot of young women want to know how can they stop their image being abused. And it's unfortunately something where I say, "I wish I could tell you this is what you have to do," but there isn't really an easy answer there. Regulators around the world have woken up to the harms caused by deepfake image abuse. Lawmakers and companies are racing to find ways to hold perpetrators accountable and to develop these technologies responsibly. I'm Claire Leibowicz, and I've worked for the past decade at the Partnership on AI leading work on AI and society and what AI means for image authenticity, the future of media, and also what it means to be human. This concept of fakery, fraud, and deception is not new, but the speed, scale, and commercialization we've seen over the past few years has made it enormously threatening to public discourse, to belief in information, and to weaponization of doubt and plausible deniability. And what are tech companies doing? Like, what's the gold standard for verifying content online now? So when you say technology companies, there's the technology companies like TikTok who are ingesting videos that people like you or me can upload and then having to decide how that gets shared. But there are also companies like OpenAI who create Sora, but they also build the models and the underlying infrastructure that really bakes in trust and verification in content. There's a big effort called the Coalition for Content Provenance and Authenticity, which is an independent effort to have this kind of consistent way of verifying content, but there's also something called digital watermarking and invisible watermarking that's somewhat separate. There's lots of different technical mechanisms, but the idea is that you can bake these signals into content that travel with it throughout the web, so that one technology company can both read that information and make decisions, like TikTok has, which is to give users control over their feeds. So there are a lot of policies about the underlying bits and ones and zeros of what's in content, but there's also a ton of work being done on AI labeling. So what goes on a video, whether it's a visible signal for an audience, that will hopefully help them make sense of this very fast-paced world of fact and fiction that's collapsing. And how effective are these technical verification methods? I mean, there is no global standard, right? So it's kind of relying on the goodwill of these companies to work. That's absolutely right. And when you say effective, there are several ways you might think about effectiveness. One is: will that actually travel over the lifespan of a piece of content? So let's say the Financial Times is using a camera that actually has this signal. When you guys post that on Instagram and post that on LinkedIn and when I share it, will that travel and be technically resilient to manipulation or forgery? That's one measure of effectiveness, but it's not the final one. The final one is what the end user understands, which is a much more complicated question. So how they label it, how they say "Was this AI generated?", that's really up for debate if those signals actually help users. So we're moving in the right direction, but they're not resilient to being removed or stripped out. And second, users interpret them in many different ways. So for instance, Meta faced a ton of backlash when they started labeling content as "Made with AI." Ostensibly they were doing what we said, right? They were adding context to content. But filmmakers and photographers were very angry that content that just used Photoshop or tweaked out a blemish was being labeled as "Made with AI" and that was perceived as punitive. And what's the state of regulation? Are there any laws governing this at the moment? In America, there's been a push towards deregulation. In the past at the federal level there was a lot of interest in adding transparency, and there were voluntary norms under the Biden administration that have now since been pushed back. California's really the regulatory trendsetter in the US for mandating this transparency. But there's also a suite of legislation on kind of adjacent interventions for deepfakery, about what does it mean to control your likeness. Right now in the United States there's a proposal, the NO FAKES Act, so that I could control my face and my voice in an era where that's increasingly manipulated, because that's fractured across the states. But in Europe, that's really where we're seeing the attention on this transparency question. The EU AI Act is working on implementing Article 50—not to get really technical with terminology—but the question that asks is how do we mandate this type of transparency so that verification becomes almost a human right across the web, such that you can know what you're seeing. There's this aura of inevitability in technology development that I think we need to push back on. And that's not to be naive about the geopolitical realities or the fact that AI is here, but I want to make sure that people—real people, whether you're in AI or not—feel they can have a say. They can post on Reddit or push back or talk to journalists about how they're using this technology. And two, those of us who have any position of influence—whether you're a journalist, a video producer, or a human rights official, especially people at the company—need to not have this sense of resignation that this is here and therefore we can't do anything about it. Because I have seen that from some of my peers, and I think there's a fine line between pragmatism and working within constraints, which we embrace, and also shrugging your shoulders and saying, "This isn't the world we want but it's here." What started as an academic experiment in cutting-edge technology has now grown into a multi-million dollar industry which is causing real harm to people. We've only just begun to grapple with the consequences of unleashing AI technologies into the wild. In the meantime, they are warping our sense of reality and identity, and as image generation models become better, it will become harder to know what is real and what is not.

JUST IN: Sec. Pete Hegseth, Gen. Dan Caine Hold A Press Briefing On The War With Iran

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·37:43 ·33.4K views · 2026-03-19
Pete Hegseth (Secretary of Defense) Dan Caine (Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff)
Nineteen days into Operation Epic Fury, U.S.
TL;DR Nineteen days into Operation Epic Fury, U.S. forces have neutralized Iran’s air defenses and surface fleet while reducing incoming ballistic missile and drone attacks by 90%. Leadership remains committed to a "total destruction" strategy of Iran’s defense industrial base...

TL;DR

Nineteen days into Operation Epic Fury, U.S. forces have neutralized Iran’s air defenses and surface fleet while reducing incoming ballistic missile and drone attacks by 90%. Leadership remains committed to a "total destruction" strategy of Iran’s defense industrial base to permanently eliminate its nuclear and regional strike capabilities.

Strategic Objectives and Conflict Status

  • Core Mission: Sec. Pete Hegseth reaffirmed that the administration’s goals remain unchanged: destroying Iran’s missiles, launchers, and defense industrial base; eliminating their navy; and ensuring they never acquire a nuclear weapon.
  • Operational Scale: To date, U.S. forces have struck over 7,000 targets. The military is transitioning from "stand-off" strikes to "stand-in" operations, meaning U.S. assets are now operating directly within Iranian airspace with total air superiority.
  • Shift in Doctrine: Hegseth emphasized that this is not a "forever war" or "nation-building" exercise like Iraq or Afghanistan, but a "laser-focused" mission to crush a direct threat to American civilization.

Degradation of Iranian Military Assets

  • Missile and UAV Suppression: Iranian ballistic missile and one-way "kamikaze" drone attacks against U.S. forces have dropped by 90% since the start of the conflict.
  • Naval Neutralization: Gen. Dan Caine reported that the U.S. has sunk or damaged over 120 Iranian navy ships and 44 minelayers.
      • Submarine Force: Hegseth noted that Iran’s entire fleet of 11 submarines has been eliminated.
      • Coastal Defense: Recent strikes utilized 5,000-pound penetrator weapons ("bunker busters") to destroy underground storage facilities for coastal defense cruise missiles.
  • Industrial Base: Strikes have targeted hundreds of factories and production lines. Hegseth noted that the IRGC senior leadership positions have become "temporary jobs" due to the high rate of successful targeting.

Tactical Execution and Force Employment

  • The "Stand-In" Fight: Gen. Caine detailed the deployment of A-10 Warthogs and AH-64 Apaches to the southern flank to hunt fast-attack watercraft in the Straits of Hormuz.
  • Strategic Bombing: B-1, B-2, and B-52 bombers are the "backbone" of the current campaign. Caine highlighted the physical and mental toll on crews, specifically the difficulty of multiple mid-air refuelings during long-range missions.
  • Refueling Logistics: Tanker crews from the Air Force Reserve and National Guard were recognized as the "unsung heroes" enabling the deep-strike capability into Iranian territory.

Diplomatic and Financial Outlook

  • Regional Coalitions: Hegseth praised Israel as an "incredible and capable partner" and noted that Gulf states (UAE, Qatar, Bahrain, Kuwait, and Saudi Arabia) have moved "squarely into our orbit" following Iranian provocations against civilian infrastructure.
  • Funding Requirements: The Pentagon is seeking a $200 billion supplemental funding package to replenish munitions stockpiles.
      • Stockpile Recovery: Hegseth attributed current stockpile challenges to the previous administration's "depletion" of resources for Ukraine, stating munitions are now being prioritized for U.S. national interests.
  • Counter-Information Warfare: The U.S. is actively working to bypass the Iranian regime’s internet blackouts and counter "AI-generated" propaganda designed to exaggerate U.S. losses.

Notable Quotes

"We've decided to share the ocean with Iran. We've given them the bottom half." — Sec. Pete Hegseth
"They have fear, of course, but their bigger fear is, 'Dear God, please don't let me screw this up'—the warrior's prayer that we all have in our souls." — Gen. Dan Caine (referring to bomber crews)
"We fight to win, and we are winning on our terms... not for nation-building or democracy promotion, but to crush direct threats to America, Americans, and our interests." — Sec. Pete Hegseth

Today I want to continue my theme of talking about members of our incredible joint force. Today I want to talk about some exceptional airmen who are engaged in the fight daily. United States Air Force, Air Force Reserve, and Air National Guard crews within our bomber force—the B-1s, B-2s, and B-52s—and the airmen on the ground who maintain and load these weapon systems. They are the backbone of America's long-range strike capability, and their contributions to Epic Fury have been decisive. Assigned to Stratcom under Admiral Rich Correll and supported by Transcom—the tanker force that we've talked about—under the command of General Randall Reed, every mission is designed to achieve overwhelming outcomes on behalf of the United States and our allies. Last weekend, I had the chance to go out and see some of our deployed bomber forces—the B-1 and B-52 crews—and I had a chance to sit down with some young aviators and maintainers and ask them, "How do you feel? Tell me about your typical day." These are crews comprised of between two and five airmen: two on the B-2s, five on the B-52s, and four in the B-1s out there. Some of these Americans are in their early or mid-20s, and we give them the gift of this incredible responsibility: hundreds of thousands of pounds of aircraft, and they take off and go do our work as required. Their mission actually begins the day prior when they're given a target or a bunch of targets, and they begin to study those targets, look at what are the effects required, what are the weapons required to get there, how will we program these weapons, what is the weather, and what is the tanker flow. It's an incredibly complex problem that we give each and every one of these crews, and they work it over the period of 24 hours prior, get some rest, and then step out the door to go fly. The last thing they do is they check all of their equipment—their oxygen masks, survival gear, load pistols—and get ready to go. They crank the jets about an hour prior to takeoff and then launch into the daylight, doing their pre-flight checks and, as the kids say, "lock in" and prepare to go to war, sometimes on a long and stressful journey. During the flight, they're surrounded by technology and capabilities, and they'll do multiple aerial refueling efforts across tankers on the way to the AOR—area of responsibility—either coming from the States or coming from a forward deployed basing. And I will tell you as a fighter pilot, getting gas is a lot easier in an F-16 than it is in a B-1, B-2, or a B-52, where you are handling this airplane. It is a physical thing, unlike a fighter that's a lot easier. And they stay on that boom for sometimes up to 30 minutes, taking hundreds of thousands of pounds of gas. It is a physical, demanding thing to take gas, and they do it multiple times on the way there and they do it multiple times on the way home. As they enter the operating area, they bring the entirety of America's joint force together to go do the things that we've tasked them to do: to put bombs on time, on target, with the proper effects. And I know they're feeling a range of emotions, but the thing the American people should take away from it is they're filled with a focus that is incredibly impressive. And they have fear, of course, but their bigger fear is, "Dear God, please don't let me screw this up"—the warrior's prayer that we all have in our souls. In the days of Epic Fury, they were shooting, as we've talked about in this room, a lot of standoff weapons. Now we've switched and rolled, as I mentioned last week, to stand-in weapons. And behind each and every one of them are incredible maintainers and weapons builders who go out there and make sure these aircraft and their weapon systems are ready to go. These airplanes are so big that they're not in hangars. They're out in the cold, out in the snow, out in the rain. The bombs are being built outside for protection and to make sure that it's safe, and they do it 24/7, 365. It's not comfortable work, but when you go spend just a minute with these incredible young Americans who are so motivated, you cannot come away from that exchange not being hopeful for America's future. It is simply awesome. And we hand these Americans, young Americans, incredible and weighty responsibility, and they deliver every single time. Quietly, with professionalism and humility, doing the things that we ask them to do. In conclusion, we will continue major combat operations. As the Secretary said, we continue to get busier. I know the Secretary and I share that we're incredibly proud of 2.8 million members of our joint force. I am personally, and we are personally grateful for the tenacity and professionalism of the brave men and women who serve inside Stratcom, Centcom, Spacecom, Cybercom, Transcom, and the rest of the joint force. And I'll close where I started. I ask today that we remember those six fallen that came home. They represent the best of our nation. For those families that are feeling the pain, know that we are with you and will remain with you. May we always prove worthy of their sacrifice. And I'll turn it back over to you, Mr. Secretary. Thank you, Mr. Secretary. Take a couple of questions. Thank you, Mr. Secretary. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Given the updated degradation and strike target numbers you laid out, how close would you say we are towards achieving the President's objectives, and what is the endgame of the operation without divulging any sensitive battle plans? Well, we wouldn't want to set a definitive time frame on that, but as we've said, we're on plan. So we're looking at those metrics very closely, relaying that to the President and the National Security Team. But I feel confident that as, again, we're more "stand-in," which means we're over the top, even further in, and we have even more of an exact sense of what we're striking and why. It is even more dynamic—meaning because the intelligence improves, we're able to more quickly identify targets when they, let's say, come out of an underground facility where they'd been hiding and able to strike it before it strikes or right after it shoots. But we're very much on plan, and that's why I want to speak to the American people here. You hear a lot of noise about widening or new missions or speculation about what we should or should not be doing. This is a clear set of objectives. The President has given us every capability we need to accomplish that. We've got the best in the world in uniform executing it on the ground. They believe in and are invested in this mission, and it will be at the President's choosing, ultimately, where we say, "Hey, we've achieved what we need to on behalf of the American people to ensure our security." So no time set on that, but we're very much on track. Absolutely. Yes, right here. Good morning, Mr. Secretary. David Zare, Real America's Voice News. What countries have been the most cooperative with us, including the Gulf states as Europe hedges? And I also wanted to ask you, the internet blackouts by the regime—it's been shut down for weeks. Is the US military playing a role in fighting against the regime blocking VPN networks and satellites and other things? Is there a role for the US military there? Obviously, on the allies and partners side, Israel from day one has been an incredible and capable partner, willing and able. There's nothing like capabilities and partners that are able to use them. The Gulf states have stepped up incredibly. In fact, Iran's sort of reckless attempt to strike civilian infrastructure and other things has brought countries who maybe would have not been as all-in as they are today squarely into our orbit. And we're proud to be defending with them, standing with them, you name it: UAE, Qatar, Bahrain, Kuwait, Saudi Arabia, and others who have been right there. And we're grateful for that kind of support. As far as the internet, we're watching the regime try to tighten its grip as much as possible. And we're obviously taking countermeasures, many of which we can't talk about here, to ensure that messaging is delivered, not just to the Iranian people writ large, but to the right audiences—certain audiences that need to hear certain things about what their fate might look like or what their choices are. So even though that regime is trying really hard to ensure the world can't see, right? They want to put out fake AI-generated images, which, by the way, sometimes our press happens to fall for, like the Abraham Lincoln on fire and turning around. These AI-generated images are meant to make it look like something's happening when the exact opposite is. So they make up fake reports and fake images to lie to their own people to try to make it look... but even then, their own people can barely receive a lot of those messages and communicate because of the blackout that they've imposed upon them. But we work around that, for sure. Sir? Thank you, Mr. Secretary. General, overnight, Iran launched missiles across the Gulf. They managed to hit the Saudi oil refinery on the Red Sea. What does it mean that they have that capability now, three weeks into the war? And Mr. Secretary, you mentioned civilian infrastructure. To that point on capabilities, you know, Iran is an energy-rich country; it could be, should be. Instead, like so many other places driven by a radical ideology, they've spent that money instead of investing in their people. That's why you had millions of Iranians protesting, because they felt like their condition and quality of life didn't match what it could be or should be. And what was the Iranian state? There's a reason we call Iran the number one state sponsor of terrorism, because they took the money they make and they invest it in tunnels and they invest it in missiles and they invest it in launchers and UAVs. Iran... you know, we're not surprised that they would indiscriminately target, and we're still seeing that. Jerry? As part of the justification for the war against Iran, the White House has laid out in fairly comprehensive fashion the Iranian regime's 47 years of terrorism aimed at the US, including pointing to the IRGC's provision of weapons and EFPs to help kill Americans in Iraq. The Pentagon in 2019 assessed Iran was responsible for the deaths of at least 603 service members in Iraq. Thank you for the question. And it's true, in a town that can have a very short memory, President Trump does not. He remembers what this Iranian regime was founded on, what their declaration has been from the beginning: "Death to America, death to Israel." They've stated that, they've intended to in every way try to accomplish that, killing Americans in any theater they can. Embassies for quite a long time. In Iraq, the deadliest weapon was the EFP, the explosively formed penetrator. I was there when that tactic and technique started to emerge, and the realization was it wasn't just jihadis putting together 155 rounds daisy-chained; it was an entire state, a country with its apparatus, able to mold steel in a way that was specifically designed to penetrate American armor and kill Americans. This was not, "Oh, maybe we send them a couple of thoughts about how to build a bomb." This was Iran specifically passing technology and wherewithal and personnel into Iraq to kill Americans specifically. I know the number's said to be 600; a lot of us think it's higher than that given the amount of capability they brought in. So they have been at war with us, whether we acknowledge it or not, for 47 years. The same goes in Afghanistan. Name the Islamist enemy, whether they are Sunni or Shia—because this is a Shia regime—Sunni or Shia, and Iran's been willing to harbor them as long as that group is willing to try to kill Americans. So the enemy of their enemy has been their friend as well in the Islamist world. So Iran, whether it's Iraq, whether it's Afghanistan, or whether it's around the world, has been targeting Americans. And what they wanted in this context, and what President Trump would not tolerate, is a regime of that nature being closer and closer to nuclear capabilities. A capability they would have said they would want to use, and we ought to believe what our enemies say they would do if they got the most dangerous weapon in the world. So it's been across every theater that Iran's been a threat to America. And sometimes we have to remind folks of that, but thank goodness we have a commander-in-chief who, on behalf of this country, has internalized that and said, "Hey, we're going to do something about it." Jerry, I do want to talk about the lessons learned question, because it's important to me. I won't share particular tactical lessons, and the KC-135 incident is under investigation. But I do want to talk about how I think about this and how we think about it. And this is something that I'm pretty passionate about. I believe the joint force has to be a very fast-learning organization, early, often, and always. And so in each case, both at the strategic, operational, and tactical level, we've charged the joint force to look at themselves and say: What was our plan? Well, first, did we have a plan? Yes or no. The answer is always yes, because we plan for literally everything. Did we execute our plan? Yes or no. If we did, did it work? If not, why not? If we didn't execute our plan but did something better, what's the lesson learned associated with that? And then how do we quickly capture those lessons after every loss or every engagement and quickly adopt those lessons and then share them across the entirety of the joint force? Inside the Joint Staff, we've tasked our J7 Directorate, under the lead of Lieutenant General Lozowski, a United States Marine, to be extremely entrepreneurial in harvesting and capturing those lessons in a proactive and not reactive way. In fact, we've got teammates from the J7 who are now spread out across the Centcom theater—both here in the States and forward—to ensure that in every opportunity, we do not miss a chance to grab that lesson and quickly accelerate it into the practice that we adopt inside the joint force. So if there's a loss, or if there's something that we can learn from, we learn it not only in the Centcom AOR, but we adapt it across all of the combatant commands, not just Indo-Pacom. And it's across all of the warfighting functions. I've asked them to look rigorously, to check our egos at the door, to remember this is about what's right, not who's right, and to be clinical around the way we look at lessons learned, because we owe it to the force to do that. Thanks for that question. Absolutely. Brown jacket at the back. Thank you, Mr. Secretary. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Jonathan Dreyer with Rocca Politics. As you've said, at the end of this war, Iran will be without a missile or drone capability, a navy, or the ability to make nuclear weapons. Global Trade Outlook 2026: press conference

World Trade Organization(42.1K) ·43:42 ·131 views · 2026-03-19
Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala (Director-General, WTO) Robert Staiger (Chief Economist, WTO)
> "The lesson for us to take...
TL;DR: Global trade growth is projected to slow significantly from a surprisingly strong 4.6% in 2025 to a modest 1.9% in 2026, as the temporary boosts from AI investment and "front-loading" of imports dissipate amid rising geopolitical risks in the Middle East. The 2026...

TL;DR: Global trade growth is projected to slow significantly from a surprisingly strong 4.6% in 2025 to a modest 1.9% in 2026, as the temporary boosts from AI investment and "front-loading" of imports dissipate amid rising geopolitical risks in the Middle East.

2026 Trade Projections and Economic Outlook

  • The 2026 Forecast: Under the WTO's baseline scenario, world merchandise trade volume growth will slow to 1.9% in 2026, down from 4.6% in 2025. A rebound to 2.6% is anticipated for 2027.
  • Normalization of Trade-to-GDP: In 2025, trade grew 1.6 times faster than global GDP (4.7% vs 2.9%). The 2026 forecast expects this relationship to return to the historical trend where trade growth closely matches GDP growth.
  • Services Sector Stability: Commercial services trade is expected to grow by 4.8% in 2026 (down from 5.3% in 2025) before picking up to 5.1% in 2027.
  • Regional Highlights:
      • Africa: Showed strong 2025 growth due to demand for fossil fuels, green transition minerals (copper, cobalt, lithium), and favorable crop conditions for cocoa and coffee.
      • Middle East: 2025 growth was driven by a rebound in energy exports.
      • Europe: Expected to be the hardest hit by potential energy price spikes due to its status as a major net importer of energy and liquefied natural gas (LNG).

Primary Drivers of the 2025 Surge

  • Import "Front-Loading": A significant portion of 2025's growth was attributed to North American companies accelerating imports early in the year to bypass expected tariff hikes in the United States.
      • This created a one-time boost in trade volume that is not expected to recur in 2026.
  • The AI Investment Boom: Surging investment in AI-enabling goods provided nearly half of the merchandise trade growth in 2025.
      • High Import Intensity: AI-related goods (chips, servers, software) have an import intensity of 70% to 90%, meaning 70–90 cents of every dollar invested goes toward imports. For comparison, construction investment has an import intensity of less than 2%.
      • Geographic Concentration: This boom primarily benefits a small group of specialists: the U.S. (design/software), Chinese Taipei and Korea (semiconductors), the Netherlands and Japan (manufacturing equipment), and China (assembly).

Geopolitical Risks and Downside Scenarios

  • The Middle East Conflict: If regional conflict persists, it could shave 0.5 percentage points off merchandise trade growth (reducing it to 1.4%) and 0.7 percentage points off services growth.
  • Energy and Food Security:
      • Strait of Hormuz: This chokepoint handles 20% of global oil consumption and 30% of global fertilizer shipments.
      • Fertilizer Impact: Disruptions to fertilizer supply threaten global food security by raising costs for farmers and prompting a shift to less productive, "low-input" crops.
  • Tariff Uncertainty: Following a U.S. Supreme Court decision affecting AEEPA tariffs, the U.S. implemented temporary Section 122 balance-of-payments tariffs (10%). Ongoing Section 301 investigations may lead to more permanent, product-specific replacements in 2026.

Resilience of the Multilateral Trading System

  • MFN Tariff Share: The share of global merchandise trade subject to statutory "Most Favored Nation" (MFN) tariffs—a key indicator of the health of the rules-based system—dropped from 80% in 2024 to 72% currently.
  • Systemic Health: Despite this erosion, nearly three-quarters of global trade still occurs under standard WTO MFN terms, suggesting the system remains functional despite significant geopolitical "battering."
  • Ministerial Conference (MC14): Leadership intends to use these figures as a "wake-up call" for members meeting in Cameroon to pursue effective reforms and maintain trade predictability.
"The lesson for us to take... is that the rules-based system may be battered—you've heard me say this before—but not broken. It's far from broken."

— Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, Director-General

"A dollar investment in AI-enabling goods [means] 70 cents to 90 cents would be spent on imports. This is exactly how the AI investment boom in 2025 helped lift import growth above GDP growth."

— Robert Staiger, Chief Economist

"The status quo is not an option... we really need to be that island of stability. Throughout all this unpredictability, the WTO rules have continued to serve for almost three quarters of goods trade."

— Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, Director-General

Good afternoon everyone. Welcome to the launch of the 2026 WTO Global Trade Outlook and Statistics. I'm Ismaila Dieng. I'm the Director of Information and External Relations here at the WTO. The report and press release are available already on our new Media Newsroom. And just a quick reminder, the report and everything that is said in this press conference are under embargo until p.m. Geneva time. We also have interpretation available in English, French and Spanish. We are joined by the Director-General, Dr. Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, and our Chief Economist, Robert Staiger. We'll start also on the podium with the economists who contributed to this report. To my right, Thomas Verbeet, who is the chief of the Integrated Database Unit. To Bob's left, we have Coleman Nee, who's the chief of forecasting. To Coleman's left, we have Barbara D'Andrea Adrian, chief of International Trade Statistics. To the extreme left, we have Marc Auboin, who is the chief of Trade and Microfinance. We'll begin with brief remarks from the DG and Bob. And after that, we'll open the floor to questions. So with that, DG, over to you. The DG will take two or three questions and leave because of other commitments. And Bob will continue with you. So DG. It's really Bob's show and the show of the team, you know. So good afternoon everyone and thank you for joining us for the launch of our Global Trade Outlook and Statistics report, which includes our latest trade projections. As you know, this year's GTOS arrives at a moment of profound economic uncertainty characterized by regional conflict in the Middle East and rapid technological change driven by AI. The report also comes on the eve of our Ministerial Conference in Yaoundé, Cameroon, where we hope it will serve as a valuable reference for members as they work to achieve cooperative and mutually beneficial outcomes whilst also protecting the most vulnerable. The report paints a picture of a global economy and a multilateral trading system facing serious challenges, but also demonstrating surprising resilience. Let me start by citing the headline figures. Under our baseline forecast scenario, world merchandise trade volume growth would slow from a stronger than expected 4.6% in 2025 to a modest 1.9% in 2026 before rebounding to 2.6% in 2027. Several factors contributed to the magnitude of the rise in 2025, including front-loading of imports in North America ahead of expected tariff hikes and surging AI-related investment. The one-time boost from front-loading is unlikely to be repeated this year while AI-related investment is expected to ease, both of which would contribute to a slower trade growth in 2026. If sustained, however, the recent rise in energy prices linked to the Middle East conflict could shave another 0.5 percentage points off merchandise trade volume growth this year, lowering it from 1.9 to 1.4%. A similar pattern is expected in services trade where a baseline forecast shows world commercial services trade volume growth slowing from 5.3% last year to 4.8% this year before picking up to 5.1% next year. Although it's generally less volatile than merchandise trade, services trade would still be negatively impacted by a prolonged conflict in the Middle East, both directly through disruptions to transport and travel and indirectly through slower GDP growth due to higher energy prices. Together, these effects would subtract 0.7 percentage points from our baseline forecast for services trade growth in 2026, reducing it from 4.8 to 4.1%. The Middle East conflict also threatens global food security since shipping disruptions and higher energy costs reduce the supply and raise the cost of fertilizer. A prolonged interruption in supply could ripple through food systems, prompting farmers to reduce their use of fertilizers and plant less input-intensive crops. Under these circumstances, it's essential to keep global food trade channels open and predictable, allowing food supplies to flow to where they are most needed. Despite significant downside risks, the trade outlook is not without its bright spots. If the Middle East conflict is short-lived and if the strong contribution from AI-enabling goods trade is repeated in 2026, world merchandise trade volume growth could be 0.5 percentage points higher than in our baseline projection, raising it from 1.9 to 2.4%. WTO members can help cushion the impact of the conflict and ease the economic burden on people worldwide by maintaining predictable trade policies and strengthening supply chain resilience. Finally, let me close by noting that this year's GTOS also has an analytical chapter focused on the share of global trade that is subject to statutory MFN tariffs. Last year at this time and taking account of trade actions through the end of 2025, we reported that this share stood at 80%, a remarkable reminder of the continuing importance of the rules-based system despite challenging times. This year we have updated our calculations to show how the share of global merchandise trade subject to statutory MFN tariffs responded to tariff actions in 2025 and through the end of February 2026. This share rose and fell during this period, finally settling at 72% currently, which is a number that we've been using. So it's very interesting that with the ups and downs we came back to 72%. For me it's an interesting story going into MC14 and I'll be a two-handed economist as usual. It's interesting because on the one hand, as economists say, there has clearly been an erosion in the rules and they've been undermined. On the other hand, it's pretty remarkable that given the severe disruptions, nearly three quarters of goods trade still crosses borders under MFN tariff terms. The lesson for us to take to Yaoundé from these numbers is that the rules-based system may be battered, you've heard me say this before, but not broken. It's far from broken. Finding our way to effective reforms of the rules in a way that can serve all members is a goal worthy of the efforts that will be needed to achieve it. Thank you. And I think my statement will be available. The statement will be made available to the press. Thank you, DG. We'll have Bob. Thank you, Ismaila. As Ismaila mentioned, I am the chief economist at the WTO, arriving in January and on leave from Dartmouth College. Thanks all for coming and thanks to Dr. Ngozi for providing a succinct overview of the report. In my remarks I will try to flesh out some of the details. I also want to take this opportunity to thank the principal authors of the report, Coleman Nee, Barbara D'Andrea Adrian, Marc Auboin, Ksenia Koloskova, for delivering an outstanding report, as well as Thomas Verbeet and Thomas Gontchar for contributing the special section on the MFN share. Many others worked as well tirelessly and we appreciate your dedication. First let me address the difficulty of forecasting in the face of yet another unexpected shock, which somehow these shocks all seem to arrive in the first quarter. The DG has already mentioned the different channels through which the conflict in the Middle East could impact global trade, not only through higher oil prices and slowing GDP growth, but also through reduced supplies and rising costs of fertilizer, threatening food security in vulnerable economies. As has been our practice in recent years, this year we present a baseline forecast that does not explicitly account for the Middle East conflict, but then we pair it with an adjusted forecast that does attempt to capture the full impact of the shock. If circumstances change dramatically, we could revisit our projections in the coming months. The report really features two headlines, but they are related. The first is the growth in merchandise world trade volume in 2025 came in at a surprisingly strong 4.6%. The second is that the forces that delivered this unusually strong growth are unlikely to persist through 2026. And in our baseline scenario, we forecast that 2026 growth in world merchandise trade will therefore fall to 1.9%. Of course the big unknown is the impact of the conflict in the Middle East on our baseline forecast. If elevated energy prices persist for the rest of the year, we expect that growth in world GDP would fall from our baseline prediction of 2.8% in 2026 to 2.5% before recovering in 2027. And we predict that this would shave half a percentage point off our baseline prediction for merchandise trade volume growth for 2026, dropping it to 1.4%. Growth would then rebound to 2.8% in 2027 since oil prices are assumed in our modeling to recede by then. A similar story holds for world commercial services trade volume, but with less variation in growth from year to year because services trade volumes tend to be more stable than merchandise trade volumes. In our baseline scenario, we forecast that 2026 growth in world services trade will decline from its 5.3% pace in 2025 to 4.8% in 2026 before recovering to 5.1% in 2027. And if energy prices remain elevated for the rest of the year as a result of the conflict in the Middle East, we expect that growth in world services trade volume would fall to 4.1% in 2026 before recovering to 5.2% in 2027. One way to see how unusual the surge in global trade growth was in 2025 is to note that economists typically expect growth in global trade in goods and services to match or slightly exceed growth in GDP. But the numbers for 2025 indicate that global trade in goods and services grew at 4.7%, much faster than the 2.9% growth in global GDP in 2025, in fact 1.6 times as fast. Our predictions for 2026 imply that the relationship between growth in global trade and growth in global GDP will fall back in line with recent historical trends. So what are the forces that delivered this unusually strong trade growth in 2025? This is where the forensic work that went into the report really shines. A first force is the front-loading of imports in North America that occurred early in 2025 in anticipation of tariff implementation by the United States in the second half of 2025. This resulted in a surge in North American imports in the early part of the year that helped to sustain the level of global trade volume for 2025. If you have the report in front of you and you turn to page 5 and look at chart 2, it gives a very clear visual of that front-loading. With the first quarter of 2025 on the right-hand side figure, it shows imports for various regions and the North American imports rise dramatically for the first quarter of 2025 before falling back and then falling below for the later quarters of the year. That's the front-loading that helped to keep import volumes high in 2025, but that's a one-off force. The second force that delivered the unusually strong trade growth in 2025 was the investment boom in AI-related goods and services. Investment is typically the second largest component of GDP after consumption, and investment is usually more import intensive than consumption. But a change in the composition of investment can alter its import content with implications for global trade flows and their relationship to GDP. As we document in the report, investment in AI-related goods and services is unusually import intensive, even for investment. For example, construction typically has a low intensity of imports, less than 2%. That means for a dollar investment in construction, 2 cents would be from imports. By contrast, analysis of the import intensity of computer equipment and of recent AI investments indicates an import intensity of 70 to 90%. That means for a dollar investment in AI enabling goods, 70 cents to 90 cents would be spent on imports. So a shift in the composition of investment away from construction and other non-AI related forms of investment toward AI-related goods and services will increase the level of imports for any given level of investment. And this is exactly how the AI investment boom in 2025 helped lift import growth above GDP growth, contributing almost half of the merchandise trade growth in 2025. And if you have the report in front of you and you turn to page 20, you will see a chart 12 that illustrates this very dramatically. The left-hand side of that chart breaks down the growth in US imports in 2025 by product category. The blue circles are positive growth and the orange circles are negative, falling imports. And the biggest blue circle is AI enabling products and that's keeping the import growth in the US high. And you'll see that the orange circles are the negative, those are the falling imports, but they all tend to be fairly close to zero, not that much fall. And the right-hand side shows the front-loading that is keeping that happening. The right-hand side figure has the breakdown by quarter of what's going on with AI enabling, those are the blue, and then everything else. And you see the big orange bar that is the front-loading of products that were anticipated to have tariffs hit and then in the first quarter and then the last three quarters those are all negative. And it's that front-loading that is masking the overall negative. So I think those two charts, 2 and 12, are really exhibit A for the forensic work that went into our explanation for why we do not expect these forces to potentially continue into 2026. I should note that many of the AI-related products come from a relatively small number of countries. These include the United States, which is known for chips design, cloud infrastructure and software; Chinese Taipei, which specializes in advanced chip manufacturing; the Republic of Korea, which manufactures memory chips and semiconductors; the Netherlands, which produces chip making equipment; Japan, which makes precision manufacturing tools; and China, which specializes in hardware assembly, servers and components. Consequently, North America, Europe and Asia are the regions most directly impacted by the AI investment boom. In addition to the duration of the Middle East conflict, also unknown is how likely it is that the investment boom in AI-related goods and services will continue at the same rate through 2026. Our baseline forecast assumes that this investment continues strongly but at a somewhat lower rate than in 2025. If this proves not to be the case and if instead the AI investment boom continues through 2026 at the rate that it did in 2025, then up to half a percentage point of additional world trade volume growth could be added to our baseline forecast. It's also important to point out that other regions are providing an important source of growth in world trade volumes for reasons not directly related to AI investment. For example, Africa's strong 2025 growth in merchandise trade volume is attributable to strong demand for fossil fuels and green transition minerals like copper, cobalt and lithium as well as to favorable conditions for crop production like cocoa and coffee. And the strong merchandise trade growth in the Middle East region was driven by a rebound in energy exports. Finally, as the Director-General noted in her opening remarks, this year's report also has an analytical chapter focused on the share of global trade that is subject to the statutory MFN tariffs. This is not a comprehensive measure of the health of the rules-based system, but it provides one indicator that can be objectively measured. Last year at this time and taking account of trade actions through the end of 2024, we reported that this share stood at 80%, a reminder of the continuing importance of the rules-based system despite challenging times. Our updated calculations in this year's report reflect the impact of tariff actions in 2025 and through the end of February 2026. As the Director-General noted, this share rose and fell during this period, finally settling at 72% currently. The takeaway is the message that the Director-General emphasized. The MFN share has declined substantially, reflecting an erosion of one of the core principles of the rules-based system. But nearly three quarters of world merchandise trade still crosses borders under MFN tariffs. Or in the Director-General's words, the rules-based system may be battered, but it is far from broken. Thank you and I look forward to taking your questions. Thank you Bob. As I said, the DG has time for two questions, so we'll start with that. Christian. Thank you very much. I'm behind the sign. DG, do you have a message to those countries that have started the chaos in the Middle East by attacking Iran? What should happen to make sure that the impact on world trade is as limited as possible? Well, obviously we're an economic institution. So I just want to say that whenever there's conflict in the world and the creation of uncertainty, it has a very difficult impact for business and for trade. So the best-case scenario is if we could if the conflict could could stop. Thank you. Thank you. Laurent. Yeah, thank you DG for the briefing. Now they have this figure of 0.5 point of percentage that might be the cost of the current war in the Middle East. So do you expect that war to be a spoiler or rather a wake-up call at the Ministerial in a few days in the way the member state will behave? Thank you. Well, even prior to the war, we needed to have a wake-up call because the world is changing so fast with technology, with geopolitics, that I think our members were recognizing that as far as multilateral trade and the institution is concerned, we really need to step up. I had said before and I was quoted, the status quo is not an option. So I maintain that. So even before the war. But I think the difficult geopolitics we live in with conflicts in several parts of the world makes it even more so that we really need to be that island of stability. You've noticed that throughout all this unpredictability, the WTO rules have continued to serve for, as we said, almost three quarters of goods trade. And this is analytically there so anyone who wants to really look at the methodology and so on can see it. I would like to ask about the effects of blocking the Strait of Hormuz, especially which regions and countries could be affected by this? And do you think that alternative routes can can be a palliation of of this blocking? Thank you. Thank you for your question. The basic economics of the income and trade patterns that would be affected by the blocking of the Strait of Hormuz tends to follow the export position or import position of the regions and countries when it comes to energy. And countries that are net energy importers will suffer from the rise in energy prices that is currently occurring. And those countries will have less income and with less income will have less ability to import. Countries that are exporters of energy and are not affected by the disruption of supply possibilities will tend to, in economic terms, tend to benefit by having higher incomes and through that higher imports. So there's a definitely a variation in the economic impacts of energy price rises on countries depending on whether they are net exporters or net importers. Importantly, this is not just about energy, it's about other things like fertilizer. And so for countries who are net importers of food and or fertilizers, the blocking of the Strait of Hormuz, while it included shipments of 20% of the world's consumption of oil, it also included about 30% of the shipments of fertilizer. And so that is a big impact that is having a potentially big impact on food importers and food security of countries across the world. So those two effects are very much important for thinking about the economic aspects of the blocking of the Strait of Hormuz. Thank you Bob. At the back of the room. Yes, thank you very much for taking my question. It's Maya Plentz from the Brief. Some analysts say that if it weren't for the massive investments on AI infrastructure in the US, the country would be in a recession. Is that sustainable do you see? And what does it mean for trade in the coming year? Thank you. Our expertise in our forecasting and here at the secretariat focuses on trade impacts of various things: trade policy, but also macro aspects. And because of that we tend to take consensus forecasts of GDP around the world and then adjust them to shocks like the oil shock. What we do not do typically is predict the overall macro impacts of shocks, whether countries in the world might go into a recession. So our report here does not address that issue. It looks at the expected impacts of oil shocks on the GDP of countries across the world based on the forces that I mentioned before, that net exporting countries who don't have the supply shocks are going to tend to have their income boosted, while net importing countries will have their income falling. But we're not looking at business cycle type implications. Thank you Bob. I have Brendan Murray, Bloomberg, on online. Brendan. Sorry. Can you hear me now? Yes, we can hear you Brendan. Okay, great. Thanks. You went over 2025 and all the disruptions that US tariffs caused and the overlaying all of that was uncertainty.
