To what extent if at all has uncertainty dissipated or are we still dealing with that kind of a headwind for 2026? In our forecasting work, the uncertainty from the 2025 tariffs is in a sense built into the structure, the economic structure going forward into 2026 that relates to movements in GNP or GDP over time and the movements in trade volumes that are observed over the years and over time. We feel that in 2026 there has not been a large aggregate tariff shock to the world. There were certainly movements in tariffs that were relatively dramatic with the US Supreme Court decision on the AEEPA tariffs, but that was very quickly replaced by the US Section 122 balance of payments tariffs at a 10% across-the-board rate mostly, and they have announced that they may raise that to 15%. But so far it has stayed at 10%. And then, as you rightly point out, the US has been launching 301 investigations. The Section 122 tariffs in the US by law can only last for 150 days. They're temporary. So the Section 301s would presumably, if they lead to positive findings of unfair trade practices, as Section 301s in the US law are all about, then result in replacement tariffs for the Section 122 temporary tariffs. And whether those replacement tariffs are higher or lower than the 122s is of course not completely known. But it seems that the expectation is that the tariffs that will ultimately be put in place are likely to be similar to the tariffs that were there under AEEPA. And that is consistent with our basic assumption that the tariff movements in 2026 are unlikely to change dramatically the overall tariffs that countries face or that the world faces. Of course, as I mentioned, certain countries will be impacted differently and certain products will be impacted differently. But for our predictions about world trade and regional trade growth, we feel that at least so far there has not been compelling evidence that the tariff movements that are new to 2026 are really the story. The story to us seems to be the AI investment and the front-loading that occurred in 2025. And that's what we're emphasizing in the report. Thank you Bob. Christian. And that prices of natural gas, liquefied natural gas, would be at $16 per million BTU. And that was also the price on March 10. And we assumed that those prices would stay at that level throughout 2026 and then return to more normal levels in 2027. So one could if one were to look today, the most recent I saw just a few minutes ago was I think the Brent crude was up to $116 per barrel, something like that. So one might say well, you know, maybe we undershot already. But what we would argue is on any given day these prices are going to move. And that's not really what's important for us. What's important is what is the average price of oil, say over the year of 2026. If it comes in around $90 a barrel, which would be the negative scenario where there was a lasting impact but maybe not at 116 rather than at 90, then our forecast will be reasonable. If in fact the oil prices go up even higher and last for the year, then of course we're going to under predict the cost. And I should say that economists at the WTO have also done some robustness tests to see how our forecast would differ if instead of the time path that we assumed for oil prices and gas prices that they stayed at a constant rate for the whole year, if instead they spiked for a few months and then came down. We feel that our forecasts are robust to that kind of variation. Where we would be undershooting things in our high energy scenario is if prices rose much more dramatically and stayed there. Then we wouldn't have the correct assumption. And that's where I mentioned that depending on how circumstances change, we could reevaluate our forecasts in the coming months to update them. President Trump participates in a bilateral meeting with Japan's PM Sanae Takaichi — 3/19/2026

TL;DR

President Trump and Prime Minister Sanae Takaichi reaffirmed the U.S.-Japan alliance during a bilateral meeting centered on the "obliteration" of Iranian military capabilities, a $40 billion joint nuclear reactor project, and strategic cooperation on energy and critical minerals.

Military Operations and Regional Security

  • Iranian Conflict Status: President Trump characterized the ongoing U.S. military "excursion" in Iran as nearly complete, claiming the Iranian navy, air force, and leadership have been "obliterated."
      • Military Metrics: U.S. officials reported a 90% interception rate of Iranian missiles and drones, attributing the success to superior U.S. defense technology.
      • Infrastructure Destruction: Trump stated the U.S. has targeted Iran’s defense industrial base and "everything but the pipes" to ensure the regime cannot quickly rebuild its military capacity.
  • Japan’s Strategic Role: Prime Minister Takaichi condemned Iran’s nuclear development and the effective closure of the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Active Diplomacy: Takaichi noted that Japanese Foreign Minister Motegi has directly engaged with Iranian leadership to demand a cessation of hostilities.
      • Security Contribution: Trump praised Japan for "stepping up to the plate" regarding maritime security, contrasting their support favorably against NATO allies.
  • Defense Spending: The Pentagon is requesting an additional $200 billion from Congress.
      • Stockpile Replenishment: Funds are intended to rebuild munitions depleted by previous aid to Ukraine and to surge production through new Raytheon and Lockheed Martin factories.
      • Procurement Policy: Trump noted that defense contractors are now restricted from using federal funds for stock buybacks, focusing instead on rapid manufacturing.

Economic Security and Energy Cooperation

  • Energy Market Stability: Takaichi presented specific proposals to stabilize global energy markets disrupted by Middle East volatility.
      • Resource Sourcing: The leaders discussed increasing Japanese purchases of U.S. oil and gas, specifically from Alaska due to its geographic proximity to Japan.
  • Resource Collaboration: A primary focus of the bilateral talks included securing supply chains for important minerals and rare earths essential for economic security.
  • Economic Milestones: Trump highlighted that the Dow Jones Industrial Average recently hit 50,000 and the S&P 500 reached 7,000, framing these as results of his administration's economic policies within a single year.

Domestic Oversight and Institutional Criticism

  • Federal Reserve Investigation: Trump leveled heavy criticism at the Federal Reserve Chairman, alleging "gross incompetence" regarding a $4 billion building renovation project.
      • Interest Rates: Trump argued the Fed is being "stubborn" by refusing to lower interest rates immediately despite the current economic climate.
      • Construction Oversight: Trump claimed he could have completed the Fed’s renovation for $25 million and suggested potential "criminality" regarding contractor costs.
  • Internal Iranian Collapse: The U.S. Treasury Secretary reported "defections at all levels" within the Iranian regime and military, suggesting the government may soon collapse from within as officials attempt to wire money out of the country.

Diplomatic Relations: China and Allies

  • Strategic Secrecy: Responding to concerns that allies were not notified before the attack on Iran, Trump defended the "element of surprise," referencing Pearl Harbor as a historical example of the efficacy of unannounced strikes.
  • China Dialogue: Takaichi emphasized that Japan remains "consistently open to dialogue" with China to maintain regional stability and protect global supply chains. Trump confirmed he plans to visit President Xi Jinping in China in the near future.

***

Notable Quotes

WATCH: JD Vance Urges Fair Treatment For US Workers In Canada Trade Ties | Auburn Hills | US News

JD Vance (Vice President, United States)
Vice President JD Vance and EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin addressed Michigan workers to highlight the administration's "American Renaissance," emphasizing aggressive deregulation, a $1,400 increase in average take-home pay over 14 months, and a "fairness-first" trade policy with allies like Canada.
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance and EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin addressed Michigan workers to highlight the administration's "American Renaissance," emphasizing aggressive deregulation, a $1,400 increase in average take-home pay over 14 months, and a "fairness-first" trade policy...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance and EPA Administrator Lee Zeldin addressed Michigan workers to highlight the administration's "American Renaissance," emphasizing aggressive deregulation, a $1,400 increase in average take-home pay over 14 months, and a "fairness-first" trade policy with allies like Canada.

Economic Growth & Manufacturing Recovery

  • Take-Home Pay Trends: Vice President Vance reported that under the current administration, the average American's take-home pay increased by $1,400 over the last 14 months.
      • This stands in contrast to a cited $3,000 loss in take-home pay during the previous Biden administration due to inflation and taxes.
  • Michigan Job Creation: The state has added over 2,000 manufacturing jobs since the beginning of the Trump-Vance term.
  • Automotive Sector Gains: U.S. vehicle sales rose by 2.4% in 2025, the largest jump since 2019.
  • Tax Policy Incentives: Vance highlighted specific legislative victories for workers:
      • No Taxes on Social Security: A key Republican-led initiative.
      • No Taxes on Overtime: Aimed at supporting hourly manufacturing employees.
      • Investment Incentives: Tax cuts for businesses that hire domestically while threatening "big fat tax increases" for companies moving jobs overseas.

Deregulation & Energy Dominance

  • The "Trump EPA" Mandate: Administrator Lee Zeldin announced that the EPA is moving away from the "binary choice" between the environment and the economy.
      • The agency aims to finalize more deregulation in one year than previous presidencies achieved across their entire terms.
  • Ending Vehicle Mandates: The administration successfully ended electric vehicle (EV) mandates to promote consumer choice and worked with Transportation Secretary Sean Duffy to reset CAFE standards.
  • Historical Deregulation: Zeldin cited the removal of the 2009 Obama endangerment finding and the elimination of "off-cycle" carbon credits as the largest acts of deregulation in U.S. history.
  • Energy Prices: Addressing rising gas prices in Detroit, Vance characterized current spikes as a "temporary blip" caused by Middle East instability. He argued that the "Energy Dominance" agenda has shielded the U.S. from the more severe energy crises facing "green energy-focused" allies.

Trade Relations & Foreign Policy

  • Canada Trade Ties: Vance asserted that Canada has "taken advantage" of the U.S. for too long regarding both trade and security.
      • Defense Spending: Vance criticized Canada for not funding its own military, effectively requiring U.S. taxpayers to subsidize Canadian security.
      • Tariff Reciprocity: The administration warned Canada that if they apply tariffs to U.S. goods, the U.S. will respond in kind to ensure "basic fairness" for American workers.
  • Iran & Nuclear Security: The Vice President reaffirmed that the administration's primary focus in the Middle East is ensuring Iran never obtains a nuclear weapon, using diplomatic or military means if necessary.
  • Administrative Alignment: Regarding the resignation of Joe Kent, Vance noted that while the President welcomes diverse opinions, staff must be fully committed to implementing the President's final decisions.

National Security & Election Integrity

  • Border & Public Safety: Vance linked the fentanyl crisis and rising crime rates to "wide-open borders" and a "war on police." He advocated for the removal of violent illegal aliens and criticized local leadership in "sanctuary cities" for non-cooperation with ICE.
  • The SAVE America Act: The administration is pushing for federal legislation to require Voter ID in all federal elections to prevent fraud and protect the sovereignty of American citizens.
  • Domestic Terrorism: Vance addressed a recent attack on Temple Israel, praising security guard Danny Phillips for his heroism and emphasizing that the government is obsessively focused on monitoring threats to the Jewish community and other citizens.

Notable Quotes

"No longer is it a binary choice. We don't have to choose between [the environment and the economy]. And in one year, we will finalize more deregulation at the Trump EPA than entire federal governments have done in the past across all agencies." — Lee Zeldin, EPA Administrator
"The American dream was sold to the highest foreign bidder for pretty much my entire life... We said everybody, whether it was on Wall Street or a foreign competitor, we were going to sell out the American dream so that somebody could make a quick buck." — JD Vance, Vice President of the United States
"If you're going to apply a tariff to our goods, we're going to start applying a tariff to your goods too. It's basic fairness... our hope with Canada is we're going to get to a point where Canada treats our workers fairly, and they're going to be a true ally and a true friend, not somebody who's taking advantage of us." — JD Vance, Vice President of the United States

You know those working families' tax cuts that Lee helped get passed, and John James and so many of our great Michiganders helped make happen. That is real money in your pocket, and for our businesses, that means they can hire more, they can create more jobs, and they can invest more in our great country. You know those working families' tax cuts mean no taxes on Social Security. If you like no taxes on Social Security, remember Republicans made it happen in Congress. If you like no taxes on overtime, because we got a lot of great overtime workers just here in this place, you ought to thank a Republican in Congress. Congressional Republicans made no taxes on overtime happen, and Democrats fought us every single step of the way. And if you happen to believe that lowering taxes for people making less than $100,000 a year and everybody else, by the way, is good policy, then again we gotta remember that it was congressional Republicans that made those victories happen. I happen to believe—I'm fundamentally an optimist about this country. I think that we have great Democrats, great Republicans, and great independents, but the Democrats in Washington, D.C. are a different breed from the Democrats that exist in most of the rest of the country. You know, I was raised by two working-class Democrats. They were patriotic. My grandfather was a union steelworker for pretty much his entire life. I don't think he would understand the Democrat party of Hakeem Jeffries and Chuck Schumer and Nancy Pelosi because they don't celebrate American victories. They don't vote for lower taxes for American workers. They don't seem to be happy when America is doing well. Fundamentally, the reason I'm here is to get us to remember. We've got an election that seems like it's very far off, but the reality is in just about six months, you're going to be asked to go to the polls and you're going to be presented a question: Do you support the party that fights for fraud and illegal immigration, or do you support the Republican party that fights for you, for lower taxes, and for good jobs right here in Michigan? USA! USA! USA! USA! USA! USA! And I want to make sure I read off things that I learned about just on the airplane that are happening to this incredible state. You know that Detroit Diesel, one of the biggest engine manufacturers, announced it was adding a third shift and recalling laid-off workers that had been laid off last year. Why do you think that happened? That happened for two reasons: because we've got tax policies that promote the investment in the American worker and because the President of the United States, for the first time in my entire lifetime, was willing to say to the rest of the world, "You're done poaching our industry; you're done poaching our jobs." If you want to build right here in the United States of America, you get a big fat tax cut. But if you're trying to undercut the wages of American workers and bring in cheap garbage from overseas, you're going to get a big fat tax increase because we're protecting our workers and we're protecting the jobs that support them. So we eliminated the EV mandate and we eliminated a lot of the taxes that made it hard for us to invest in our communities. We had a President of the United States who was willing to go to war against those foreign companies and those foreign countries who were undercutting the wages of American workers, and that is how you get the results that we have seen just over the last 14 months. I'll tell you because I see him every day—I talked to him this morning before I hit the road to come to Michigan—the President of the United States is proud of this record, but he is more impatient than any person I have ever met. One of the things I love about him is he will constantly tell Lee, me, and the entire economic team, "Well, let's celebrate our achievements of course, but what have we done today?" "What have we done in the last week? What have we done in the last month to keep this progress going?" Now, there's a statistic I'm gonna toss out at you, but I think all of you have felt this statistic in your hearts. Because under the Biden administration, the average American worker lost $3,000 in take-home pay. That was a combination of two things: higher prices, that terrible inflation problem that we had under Joe Biden's leadership, but it was also because of higher taxes. So every single one of us got about an average of $3,000 poorer while Joe Biden and the Democrats ran Washington, D.C. That is a very, very bad statistic. Now the good news is, over the last 14 months under Donald Trump's leadership, the average American has actually increased their take-home pay by about $1,400. Now that's a good thing. That $1,400 is money in your pocket. But what it also means is that a lot of our families are still struggling because of that terrible hole that Joe Biden and the Democrats put us in. For so many of us, the question in November is going to be very simply: Do we support the people who made us hundreds and hundreds of dollars richer, or do we support the people who made us hundreds and hundreds of dollars poorer? Let's support the people who made us richer, more prosperous, and more secure, and that's the congressional leadership in Washington, D.C. Because here's the thing: I landed—I don't even remember which airport we landed in—but it was about a 20-minute drive from there to here. I was looking around and I was thinking to myself—and again, this is why even though I'm an Ohio State guy, I love this state—it reminded me so much of my hometown. You've got these beautiful old homes that I saw, places that harken back to an era of American greatness when we built these beautiful buildings. Some of them looked amazing and some of them, let's be honest, looked like they were struggling. I drove through some areas that were clearly struggling. You know what I realized? That the American dream was sold to the highest foreign bidder for pretty much my entire life. I'm 41 years old, and we said everybody, whether it was on Wall Street or a foreign competitor, was going to sell out the American dream of the people right in this room. They did it so that somebody could make a quick buck, or so that some foreign competitor or some foreign country could get rich off of the infrastructure that the United States of America had built over 250 proud years. What I realized driving through this area is that the American Renaissance—ladies and gentlemen, $1,200 richer in 14 months is a lot—but there is so much more that we can do together. There is so much more to be built, so much more to be reclaimed, and so much more to be made anew in the United States of America. But it's not going to happen by going back to the failed policies of Joe Biden and Nancy Pelosi; it's going to be by doubling down on the policies of Donald J. Trump and the people who love this country. In the same way that the American dream wasn't sold out in one year, it's going to take us longer to rebuild it. What is really at stake in this election in November is fundamentally this: we've done so much good and we've built so much, but the Democrats threaten to take us straight back to where we were just a few short years ago. We all know the economic statistics. We all know the fact that we felt it in our pockets—that everything from groceries to our home electricity bill was going up at a level that it hadn't gone up in 40 years under Joe Biden. But it's not just that, because the American dream, my friends, as you all know, it's not just about going out and being able to buy something, as important as that is. It's not just about the economics, the dollars and cents, though of course we demand that our citizens become more prosperous. It's also about peace and security; it's about being safe in your own home. It's about what I think of as a classic American idea that just because you grew up in a poor family like mine doesn't mean you shouldn't be able to walk down the street in safety and security in your own country. That is what Joe Biden and his leadership and Nancy Pelosi and all these clowns in Washington tried to take away the most from us. Where do you think the fentanyl that flooded our streets and killed hundreds of thousands of our citizens came from? It came from Joe Biden's wide-open border. Where do you think the highest crime rates and the highest murder rates came from during that administration? They came from the fact that we went to war on the police rather than on the criminals who committed crimes. Where do you think the fact that we had elevated murder rates not just in our big cities but in our small towns came from? We let in hundreds of thousands of violent criminals into the United States of America. So when I talk about the American dream, I mean number one: that I want you to have a good job that pays you a good wage and allows you to put food on the table, but also take a couple of nice vacations every year. But what I also mean is that we need to get the people who are making our cities less safe the hell out of our country and focus on American citizens first. As much as we have achieved and as proud as I am of what we've done over the last 14 months, the Democrats threaten to take us right back to where we were the last time that they had control of the government. Think about the State of the Union address. It feels like forever ago; I think it wasn't even a month ago. As you all know, I have a very unique perspective on the State of the Union address, right? I'm the one person—the President's obviously talking, and I guess me and Mike Johnson, the Speaker of the House, are the two people who just get to see the Congress. We don't have anything to say; you stand up and sit down probably 150 times. But it gave me a perspective on what's going on in our capital more than what's going on in our country, because I realized that there is such cowardice in Washington, D.C., in the political leadership there. I've never seen anything like it. You may remember this line—if you don't I'll repeat it for you—but the President said something to the effect of, "If you believe that the government ought to put American citizens first rather than illegal aliens, stand up and cheer." Every Republican and like three or four Democrats stood up and cheered. But what was funny is not just that the Democrats were sitting on their hands; it's that they were all looking around, like, "What do we do here?" What kind of a person is elected to serve in congressional leadership and has to look around at their peers rather than stand up and clap for the basic idea that the American government should be fighting for you? What kind of a person can't stand and clap for a six-year-old girl who was hugging her father because she was a little nervous in the public spotlight, when we were celebrating this six-year-old girl because she had survived a criminal attack from an illegal alien who shouldn't be in this country? And the Democrats couldn't stand and cheer at that. The thing that I took away from that is much more than any political issue or policy issue. I can stand up here and proudly say that we shut the border, we lowered your taxes, and we have the lowest murder rate in history, and that is something I'm very proud of. But you know what I'm more proud of? That I am part of a political movement that is fighting for each and every single person in this room. It doesn't mean you're always going to agree with us; it doesn't mean you're going to agree with everything that congressional Republicans do in Washington, D.C. But what I do know is that they wake up and they think, "How can we best serve the people of this community and the people of the United States of America?" That is the biggest dividing line, and it breaks my heart to say it. The biggest dividing line in our politics is not tax policy or regulatory policy or even immigration, as much as you and I care about all these things. The biggest dividing line in our country is that we have in Washington, D.C. one political movement that is dedicated to fraud, to illegal immigration, and to making sure that this country is safe for them rather than safe for us. I would love to live in a country where when the President of the United States says it is the duty of the American government to look after American citizens first, everybody—Democrat and Republican—stands up and cheers. But my simple request is that until we get to that moment, until the Democrats can cheer for the idea that the government fights for you, do not send those crazy people or any more of them to Washington, D.C. Support the people who fight for you, and that is Donald J. Trump and the entire leadership of this party. Okay, so I've said everything or at least most of what I want to say here, but I want to just give one note of shout-out to our fellow Americans in this community who've suffered a little bit in the last couple of weeks, and then I'll take some questions from the reporters. I believe that we're only about half an hour or so away from the facility, Temple Israel, the synagogue that was attacked by a terrorist just—well, not even a week ago, I believe. I think that all of us ought to keep these folks in our prayers because when something happens to any member of our American family—and this particular incident happened to Jewish members of our American family—it is something that all of us have to stand up and say is disgusting and unacceptable. We're not going to tolerate that in the United States of America. I hope all the folks from Temple Israel know that we're praying for them and thinking about them. From the President of the United States, the Vice President, and everybody else, we love you and we're proud of how you've handled this particular situation because it is tough. We'll take a few questions from the reporters, but what I'd like to do is start out with the local reporters first and then, if we have time, we'll get to the national reporters after that. Okay, go ahead guys. "Hi, Mr. Vice President, Brett Kast with WXYZ here in Detroit. Thank you for taking questions. We're seeing gas prices here climbing at home, in particular here in Metro Detroit with what's going on with Iran. What is the administration doing to help keep gas prices down?" Well, you know the President of the United States has been crystal-clear about this. Look, gas prices are up, and we know they're up and we know that people are hurting because of it. We're doing everything that we can to ensure that they stay lower. I will say—the President said this and I certainly agree with it—this is a temporary blip. What happened under the Biden administration is that gas prices were high for four years. Gas prices are higher right now and frankly they're not even as high as they were during certain parts of the Biden administration because of what's going on in the Middle East. It's not going to last forever. We're going to take care of business, we're going to come back home, and when that happens you're going to see energy prices come back down to reality. But in the meantime, we got a problem. We know that we have a problem, and we're doing everything that we can to address it. A couple of things—a couple of steps that we've taken just in the last couple of weeks—is that we actually worked with a lot of our allies all over the world. They are suffering from this frankly more than we are because, unlike the United States where we've pursued an energy dominance agenda, you've got a lot of people all over the world who have focused on green energy scams and they're hurting a lot more than we are. So as much as we've gotta focus on getting these gas prices down, the reality is overseas they're feeling it far worse than we do because we have taken the steps to protect our energy economy. For example, what we did working with these allies is we released hundreds of millions of barrels out of all these petroleum reserves so that we could put some downward pressure on prices. We're working on a number of things; in fact, a couple of things that I think will be announced in just the next 24 to 48 hours. I don't want to get ahead of the President on that. We recognize this is an issue, we are fighting against it, and we promise that when this conflict draws to a close, and when this operation draws to a close, we're going to see those energy prices come back down to reality because that's what the President promised to do. He delivered an energy dominance agenda that has made us much more secure in the face of these things. Yeah, we've got a rough road ahead of us for the next few weeks, but it's temporary. 'THEY ARE IN CHAOS': Bessent details internal COLLAPSE of Iran’s regime

Fox Business(3.3M) ·23:24 ·251.7K views · 2026-03-19
Scott Bessent (Treasury Secretary) Maria Bartiromo (Host, Fox Business)
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent reports that the Iranian regime is in a state of internal collapse and leadership "decapitation" following three weeks of U.S.
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent reports that the Iranian regime is in a state of internal collapse and leadership "decapitation" following three weeks of U.S. military strikes. To stabilize surging global energy prices, the Treasury is executing a "physical intervention"...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent reports that the Iranian regime is in a state of internal collapse and leadership "decapitation" following three weeks of U.S. military strikes. To stabilize surging global energy prices, the Treasury is executing a "physical intervention" by unsanctioning millions of barrels of Russian and Iranian "oil on water" to flood the market and offset supply disruptions.

Iran Conflict and Regime Status

  • Regime Collapse: Bessent asserts the Iranian leadership has been "decapitated" and is in a state of chaos, likening the current situation to "Hitler’s bunker" with senior officials dead or unable to communicate.
  • Operation Epic Fury: The military campaign has escalated into its 20th day, with the U.S. conducting its largest night of strikes recently.
  • Targeting Strategy: The U.S. is intentionally avoiding Iran’s energy infrastructure (specifically Kharg Island’s 90% export nexus) to preserve assets for a post-regime Iranian population. Strikes are strictly limited to military assets on the island.
  • Internal Unrest: Economic pressure, including a major bank failure and hyperinflation in late 2025, has led to widespread domestic defections and civil unrest within Iran.

Global Energy and Market Stabilization

  • Physical Market Intervention: The Treasury is not intervening in financial futures but is creating "excess supply" by unsanctioning oil currently held in "floating storage."
      • Oil on Water: 130 million barrels of Russian oil and roughly 140 million barrels of Iranian oil are being released to the market.
      • Supply Buffer: This combined 260+ million barrels is intended to provide approximately three weeks of supply to offset the temporary closure of the Strait of Hormuz.
  • SPR Release: A coordinated Strategic Petroleum Reserve (SPR) release of 400 million barrels was approved last week—the largest in history.
  • Energy Independence: Bessent noted a significant divergence between WTI (U.S.) and Brent (European) oil prices, attributing lower U.S. prices to domestic energy independence and the U.S. status as a net exporter.

Geopolitical Alliances and Counter-Adversaries

  • Japan’s Role: The Prime Minister of Japan is meeting with President Trump to discuss global energy security. Japan is expected to release its own petroleum reserves and potentially use its navy—noted for elite mine-sweeping capabilities—to assist in the Strait of Hormuz.
  • China’s Complicity: China is criticized for being an "unreliable supplier" and the primary financier of Iranian terrorism, previously purchasing 90-95% of Iranian oil at a discount.
      • Strategic Decoupling: Bessent emphasized the need for a "strategic decoupling" as China has halted refined product exports (like jet fuel) during the conflict.
  • European Hesitation: Bessent expressed disappointment in European allies who rely heavily on the Strait for LNG but have been slow to join the maritime coalition.

Domestic Economy and the Federal Reserve

  • Economic Outlook: Despite the war, Bessent maintains a forecast for "non-inflationary growth" above 3% for 2026, stating the U.S. entered the conflict from a position of economic strength.
  • Fed Leadership Crisis: Chairman Jay Powell has signaled he will not leave the Federal Reserve board—even after his term as Chair expires May 15—until an investigation into his conduct is concluded with "transparency and finality."
  • Succession Stalled: The confirmation of Kevin Warsh as Powell’s successor is currently held up in the Senate by Senator Thom Tillis, pending the results of the Powell investigation.
  • Tariff Policy: The administration is not changing its tariff strategy or Section 301 investigations due to the war; however, Bessent clarified there are currently no tariffs on nitrogen-based fertilizers.
"The regime is in trouble. I said last week... they're in Hitler's bunker and Hitler's dead. Well, I can tell you as of this week Himmler and Goering are dead too. The leadership has been decapitated." — Scott Bessent
"In essence, we will be using the Iranian barrels against the Iranians to keep the price down... We have allowed Iranian oil to continue out of the Gulf [to non-Chinese markets]... that oil will go up to a market price and it will end up in places other than China." — Scott Bessent
"I have no intention of leaving the board until the investigation is well and truly over, with transparency and finality." — Jay Powell
"We have to get the American people... back in the mindset of: there is no prosperity without security. Any prosperity without security is an illusion." — Scott Bessent

Powell discusses Fed's decision to keep rates unchanged amid pressure from Trump | full video

Face the Nation(780.0K) ·44:41 ·4.8K views · 2026-03-19
Jerome Powell (Chair, Federal Reserve)
The Federal Reserve is maintaining current interest rates as it navigates a "difficult balance" between persistent inflation—driven by tariffs and energy shocks—and emerging downside risks in the labor market.
TL;DR The Federal Reserve is maintaining current interest rates as it navigates a "difficult balance" between persistent inflation—driven by tariffs and energy shocks—and emerging downside risks in the labor market. While median projections still suggest rate cuts later this...

TL;DR

The Federal Reserve is maintaining current interest rates as it navigates a "difficult balance" between persistent inflation—driven by tariffs and energy shocks—and emerging downside risks in the labor market. While median projections still suggest rate cuts later this year, Chair Jerome Powell emphasized that policy is not on a preset course and remains strictly data-dependent.

Monetary Policy and Interest Rate Projections

  • Rate Maintenance: The Federal Open Market Committee (FOMC) decided to keep the federal funds rate unchanged to continue monitoring risks to both sides of its mandate (stable prices and maximum employment).
  • Summary of Economic Projections (SEP): The median participant projects the federal funds rate will end 2024 at 3.4% and 2025 at 3.1%.
      • Internal Shift: While the median remained unchanged from December, several individual participants moved their forecasts toward fewer rate cuts (shifting from two cuts to one).
  • Policy Flexibility: Powell stressed that the Fed will make decisions on a "meeting-by-meeting" basis, noting that if progress on inflation stalls, the projected rate cuts will not occur.

Inflation Drivers: Tariffs, Energy, and Services

  • Tariff Impact: A significant portion of core inflation (estimated between 0.5% and 0.75%) is attributed to tariffs.
      • One-Time Effects: The Fed expects goods inflation to drop as the "one-time" price increases from tariffs wash through the system, a process that typically takes about a year.
  • Energy Shocks: While the Fed traditionally "looks through" temporary energy price spikes, Powell noted this is more difficult now because inflation has been above the 2% target for five years, risking unanchored inflation expectations.
  • Non-Housing Services: This sector remains "frustrating," having moved sideways for a year despite a loosening labor market. The Fed is looking for this area to show progress alongside housing and goods.

The Labor Market and "Stagflation" Concerns

  • Job Creation Realities: Powell acknowledged that after adjusting for "overcounting," private-sector job creation is effectively at zero.
      • Supply-Side Factors: This zero-growth equilibrium is linked to changes in immigration policy and low growth in the labor force.
  • Downside Risks: The Fed now views risks to the labor market as being on the "downside," while risks to inflation remain on the "upside."
  • Rejection of "Stagflation": Powell explicitly rejected the term "stagflation" to describe the current economy.
      • Context: He reserved the term for 1970s-style conditions (double-digit unemployment and high inflation), noting current unemployment remains near historic lows.

Productivity, AI, and Future Growth

  • Growth Revisions: Economic growth forecasts were revised upward slightly to 2.0%, driven by "meaningfully higher" productivity over the last several years.
  • AI’s Role: Powell noted that current productivity gains are likely due to pandemic-era efficiencies rather than generative AI.
      • Neutral Rate: In the short term, AI investment (building data centers) may actually push inflation and the neutral interest rate higher before providing long-term disinflationary supply benefits.

Institutional Governance and Independence

  • Chair Succession: If a successor is not confirmed by May 15, Powell will serve as "Chair pro tem." He stated he has no intention of leaving the Board of Governors until the current investigation is concluded with "transparency and finality."
  • Political Independence: Powell defended the Fed’s independence as the "accepted standard practice" necessary for price stability, noting it has broad bipartisan support in Congress.

***

Notable Quotes

"We are balancing these two goals in a situation where the risks to the labor market are to the downside... and the risks to inflation are to the upside. We feel like where we are now is just kind of on that borderline." — Jerome Powell
"I would reserve the term stagflation for a much more serious set of circumstances... This is nothing like what they faced in the 1970s." — Jerome Powell
"The U.S. economy has really been just doing pretty well through a lot of significant challenges over the past few years. It’s been amazing to see." — Jerome Powell
"Independence is what allows us to do our jobs... It’s critical that we have that so that we can do the things when we need to do them to preserve price stability." — Jerome Powell
Evaluation of the potential effects on the economy. We will continue to monitor the risks to both sides of our mandate. We are well positioned to determine the extent and timing of additional adjustments to our policy rate based on the incoming data, the evolving outlook, and the balance of risks. In our SEP, FOMC participants wrote down their individual assessments of an appropriate path of the federal funds rate under what each participant judges to be the most likely scenario for the economy. The median participant projects that the appropriate level of the federal funds rate will be 3.4 percent at the end of this year and 3.1 percent at the end of next year, unchanged from December. As is always the case, these individual forecasts are subject to uncertainty, and they are not a committee plan or decision. Monetary policy is not on a preset course, and we will make our decisions on a meeting-by-meeting basis. To conclude, the Fed has been assigned two goals for monetary policy: maximum employment and stable prices. We remain committed to supporting maximum employment, bringing inflation sustainably to our 2 percent goal, and keeping longer-term inflation expectations well anchored. Our success in delivering on these goals matters to all Americans. We at the Fed will continue to do our jobs with objectivity, integrity, and a deep commitment to serve the American people. “We've lived through 3 ice ages - here's what we learned” - how Indigenous wisdom can help us all

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·28:24 ·558 views · 2026-03-19
Deen Sanders (Leader, Worimi Nation) Fawn Sharp (Leader, Quinault Indian Nation)
Indigenous peoples represent only 6% of the global population but steward 60% of the world's landmass; their ancient knowledge of adaptation and relational governance offers a critical blueprint for transitioning global economies from extractive models to sustainable "living systems.
TL;DR Indigenous peoples represent only 6% of the global population but steward 60% of the world's landmass; their ancient knowledge of adaptation and relational governance offers a critical blueprint for transitioning global economies from extractive models to sustainable...

TL;DR

Indigenous peoples represent only 6% of the global population but steward 60% of the world's landmass; their ancient knowledge of adaptation and relational governance offers a critical blueprint for transitioning global economies from extractive models to sustainable "living systems."

The Scale and Resilience of Indigenous Stewardship

  • Disproportionate Impact: Indigenous peoples manage the majority of the world’s most biodiverse and environmentally rich landmasses (60%), effectively outperforming all other conservation models.
  • History of Adaptation: Deen Sanders (Worimi Nation) highlights that Aboriginal Australians have survived three separate ice ages and a 20% loss of physical landmass, proving that Indigenous cultures are built on successful climate adaptation over 100,000 years.
  • Ancient Governance: Sanders notes that despite having 500 separate nation groups in Australia with distinct languages, there is no historical evidence of war, prisons, or borders, suggesting Indigenous systems of "relational regulation" are superior to Western models for managing shared resources.

Reimagining Capital and Economic Metrics

  • Externalities and Debt: Sanders argues that the "profit motive" of extraction relies on treating nature as an externality. He asserts that current corporate and state models are effectively "bankrupt" if they were forced to pay the true environmental cost of their operations.
  • Interconnected Systems: Fawn Sharp (Quinault Indian Nation) emphasizes that Western finance often views nature as a dead commodity, whereas Indigenous knowledge treats it as a living system. For example, a copper mine cannot be evaluated without accounting for its impact on water and the surrounding ecosystem.
  • Shifting Financial Frameworks: Indigenous leaders are advocating for a "dual time horizon":
      • Short-term: Innovating within current systems to value natural capital.
      • Long-term: Moving toward an "Indigenous finance platform" that uses metrics based on ecological thresholds rather than just profit maximization.

Practical Policy and Financial Successes

  • The Washington State Model: Fawn Sharp cites the Climate Commitment Act in Washington State as a prime example of Indigenous leadership.
      • Direct Revenue: The act mandates that 10% of all carbon revenues be dedicated directly to tribal nations.
      • Capital Leverage: Since its inception, the act has driven $1 billion into the state treasury for local communities and Indigenous nations.
  • Strategic Investment Tools: Sharp envisions the creation of "Deep Green" and "Deep Blue" bonds—premium investment vehicles that allow the private sector to deploy capital directly into sovereign tribal nations to fund restoration and sustainable development.
  • Generational Accountability: Sanders introduces the concept of "Mabulubul" (Next Seven Again), a framework where every economic and social decision is weighed against its impact on the next seven generations.

A Universal Invitation to "Place"

  • Shared Identity: Sanders asserts that "everyone is indigenous to somewhere," arguing that the modern world has lost its "deeper relationship to place." Indigenous wisdom is not an exclusive "diversity play" but an invitation for humanity to reconnect with the land it depends on.
  • The Role of "Country": In Indigenous philosophy, "Country" (the land) is the teacher, and humans are merely its spokespeople. Survival depends on shifting from a "power grab" for resources to a "shared responsibility" for the environment.

***

Notable Quotes

"Indigenous people, despite being only 6% of the global population, have access and responsibility for 60% of the world's landmass... We might be small in number, but we have great knowledge to share, great power to share."

— Deen Sanders, Co-chair, Global Future Council on Natural Capital

"The values that we hold as Indigenous peoples are simply inherently incongruent with a system that by design is intended to maximize profit at the expense of nature... We are in a day of reckoning."

— Fawn Sharp, Quinault Indian Nation and GFC Member

"We have sought through economic mechanisms to make [nature] an externality that we're not responsible for today. That's just simply not true. We're always paying for it in the future. It's a debt to our grandchildren."

— Deen Sanders, Worimi Nation

Guppar dolphin is my nation totem. But I'm also from Warragul Natta, freshwater people. I was born on the mountains, born in freshwater country. People did bad things of course, as human beings do, but we had systems of governance and relational regulation that governed how we connected with each other and met our responsibilities. So we think this beautiful story in there about 500 separate nations, by the way with different languages and different cultures, different song, different story. When you have that level of richness and depth and difference, but nonetheless shared purpose, I think that's the story of future for the rest of the world. Yes, thank you, I too appreciate that question. I come from the Quinault Indian Nation, it's a tribal nation located in Western Washington, about three hours west of Seattle. Of course, prior to the United States, there were thousands of indigenous peoples throughout North America and through colonization, we started forming tribal governments as a result of US policy. But to describe our people as politically distinct, that's just one way of describing tribal nations in the United States. We too have lived in place for centuries. The place where I come from is absolutely beautiful and pristine. I live, personally live right where Lake Quinault meets the Quinault River. One can travel from glacier to ocean and see absolutely no development. We have lakes, we have beaches, we have a rainforest, and it's absolutely stunning and beautiful because our citizens have kept and honored the teachings that we've had, that we have to protect our resources. And once the United States arrived, we began to mount aggressive campaigns to defend our very homelands. There was mass and widespread clear-cutting of our forests. My nation sued the United States all the way to the United States Supreme Court to protect our timbered lands. And so we not only protect and honor the teachings of our ancestors to respect nature, we're also taught that the salmon cannot get out of the rivers and defend themselves. We are the guardians of all of our relatives in the ocean and the lakes and that, you know, our relatives that fly. And so we have this very beautiful place because we've honored teachings and because we aggressively defend those resources from colonization and others who seek to disrupt it. So despite being very diverse all over the world, there are indigenous peoples, you can and do come together at events like this. You're a voice, you're advocates for certain issues. You've both mentioned the environment there, would you say that's one of the key areas where indigenous voices can help the rest of us, the whole world, improve things? I mean can you give us any specific example—you've given us one of a local one, that's very interesting—but when it comes to things like climate change or protecting nature maybe in other parts of the world where you are not based, do you think you're a force that can actually have some impact? In our country alone, people have lived through three separate ice ages. We've seen our country shrink in physical landmass by 20%, and our cultures and systems move and adjust to that. So there were stories of adaptation there. There were stories of how do we respond to the change in those environments. And these are messages for the future. But there's also the idea of there is a general narrative that indigenous people are people of the land, and as Fawn spoke to, there's deep knowledge in that. But you don't survive for 100,000 years in 500 separate nations without other tools: tools of governance, tools of economics, and trade and exchange. These are also, I think, stories for the future of the wider West that we weren't allowed to tell, we haven't been invited to tell. And I think there are genuine stories about that. What we do know is that there is a shared principles around those things globally. And we love catching up as indigenous people, indigenous leaders around the world, because while the West of the world is frankly crumbling in its relationships, we are growing stronger. Our connections and relationships, the data tells us that indigenous people, despite being by the way only 6% of the global population and disparate numbers in different countries—we're only 3% in my country, 3 to 4%—but we have access and responsibility for 60% of the world's landmass. And most of that is the biodiverse and environmentally rich environments. So we have great responsibility. We might be small in number, but we have great knowledge to share, great power to share. So there's a universal story about that future that brings us together in conversation. Yes. Yes, and I would add to what Deen pointed out by suggesting that our ancestors have long foretold of a day of reckoning, when the world is on a trajectory that simply isn't sustainable. I think you're absolutely right in your inquiry, do we have something to offer to teach the rest of the world? Yes, through centuries of knowledge and prophecy that at some point the world was not going to be able to sustain a life-way and we're at that point now, we are in a day of reckoning. And I also think that to Deen's point about the disproportionate share, population versus biodiversity, I think those numbers in and of themselves prove that we can outperform everyone else in our life-ways by maintaining what's left, the biodiversity that's left. And so I often flip those numbers to say we are outperforming everyone else by a very high percentage in our land stewardship. And certainly the principles that have maintained that stewardship are lessons that we can teach the rest of the world. And one of the biggest challenges we have is directly accessing finance, directly accessing capital. And that's been the call of indigenous people for decades, that we are on the front lines of climate change, we are disproportionately impacted, and we have little to no resources. But yet we struggle to maintain access to capital. And so I think the beauty of our council, the Council on Natural Capital, is indigenous people can come to these conversations and we can say: we do not have access to capital, we do not have access to finance, but what we do have access to is nature. And with our access to nature, we can bring our teachings into these conversations and help guide, influence, and quite frankly, lead the world in how to maintain financial metrics that really reflect these are living systems. Nature is not just a commodity that through transactions it's traded. These are living systems that are relational. You can't look at a copper mine without thinking about water, and all of these systems are interconnected. And those are the very teachings that we are seeking to not only bring to the conversation but develop tools for the rest of the world to know and understand that when you value nature, there are so many other metrics that need to be brought into the conversation. I put to him—I ask the same question to you though—we all know nature has a value, massively important, you know vital value, we would die without it. As custodians of the land, what can you practically say to global economies that are driven by the profit motive of extracting and exploiting the land, often for perfectly good reasons to feed our children and to develop? But how can we do that? What advice have you got to help countries and companies become nature positive, to leave the nature in better state? What's the practical... because I think everyone would agree with it, but how do you implement that? Who's first? Look, it's a big question Robin. But I would argue that in fact that it does have a financial cost. And that most people haven't calculated that. That's the truth of it. We have the work of Dasgupta speaking about this idea of how if everybody paid for what they used, most corporations would in fact be bankrupted by the truth of those costs. As would most nation states. So I think, I mean we have a number of answers and we're working on that as part of this larger conversation that we're excited to be part of with our Global Future Council on Natural Capital, trying to as Fawn spoke to, trying to bridge that relationship between nature and capital. But I want to be really clear: it absolutely has a financial cost in every instance. We just haven't bothered to pay for it. We have sought through economic mechanisms to make that an externality that we're not responsible for today. That's just simply not true. We're always paying for it in the future. It's a debt to our grandchildren. In my culture we have a term, "mabulubul" in Gathang language—my language, we have a term "mabulubul" which means "next seven again." Which means we are always thinking about the next seven generations of responsibility. And I know that they will pay for these things. The cost will be borne by them, of what we do today, how we sit here today. So I'm animated, I'm responsible in the conversations today to that. My job is to invite you into that. It's not just what indigenous people have to do. To Fawn's point we have a job to do, we have responsibilities to meet. But we want to invite the rest of the world into that too. And I think that's the shift. The shift is how do we invite everybody into that? The good news, and you spoke to it, is that most conservationists, most forward-thinking people realize that the knowledge sits here, as it has always sat here. It's extraordinary to read about our cultures and every book by every frankly, if I can be honest, every white conservationist telling stories about indigenous knowledge. Let the indigenous people tell the stories. Let us control the economics of it, and we suddenly will get a rebalancing. What we're seeking to do in our conversations, to simplify it, is make this not just a nation state conversation because I think that's the challenge for many governments. But to make it a corporate conversation. A corporate capital conversation, but also make it a family dinner—dining room table conversation. Until we understand the cost of these things around our dining room tables across families around the world, we will never win this particular fight. If we leave it to governments, that's not the right answer. We've got to make this a real lived reality of economic cost for everybody. And it's absolutely a financial cost. And I would add to that—thank you Deen—I just, I get inspired when I listen to Deen. He really makes me think about the fact that we are on a dual time horizon here. We need to adopt short-term innovation that includes our values, but we also need long-term systemic change. And through this last year, there was a moment in time where it just occurred to me that the values that we hold and carry as indigenous peoples are simply inherently incongruent with a system that by design is intended to maximize profit at the expense of nature. These are two systems that are just simply inherently incongruent. And so the world's best effort to bring nature into the financial systems and to commodify and realize value that goes beyond extraction, I think we need to not only look at innovative examples today, but what's beyond 2030? How are we going to be reimagining metrics? Imagine financial metrics and investment strategies that follow ecological thresholds. Imagine investment strategies that actually value human life in relation to the maximization of profitability. And so I think these are all things that the current system, I call it the status quo, must look at with intention and strategy—how to bring in indigenous values into every aspect of valuing nature. I think the world also needs to understand that indigenous peoples are reimagining a financial system from our own perspective. We're reimagining systems, entire systems where imagine an indigenous finance platform or exchange where investors could go directly to indigenous communities to deploy capital at blended scale. Whether it is public, private, or philanthropic finance. And in those systems, you have metrics where you're not trying to mainstream living systems and valuation, but these are part of by design where we are the architects of financial metrics that include all things living. That include the spiritual dimensions of our relationship not only with humanity and the natural world, but with our creator and our teachings. And so I think those are the things that we offer at this moment in time and as I said we might not have access to finance and capital, but we do have access to this incredible amount of biodiversity throughout the world and we do have—I describe it as a mantle of leadership that is—it was achieved with a heavy price. The mantle of leadership that we carry was at great expense to our citizens, to our relatives and to our homelands. And so I feel very strongly that we know that this is a day of reckoning. As Deen pointed out, all the systems around us are crashing and proving that they're failing humanity, they're failing the financial system itself, they're failing the world, they're failing our environment, and the world is looking for other solutions. And it's a moment in time where we are rising and our teachings are rising and leading the rest of the world, but we have to really open up our heart, our mind, and our spirit to engage in these conversations. This is my last question, but it's a big one and the short version is this: is anyone listening to you? That things have improved in a way that might not have happened had you not been involved in that conversation. The original short version of that question was: is anyone listening? Yes, thank you. The first part of your question when you raised it, are we visible? And I thought to myself, "wow, that's quite a disconnect because what I'm describing is definitely the real world." And the place is the one example I would use that we were not only visible, but we made a difference. In the state of Washington, there was an effort to hold polluters accountable and price carbon. And we co-led an initiative, secured 300,000 signatures, put this initiative on the ballot; it was called I-1631. The Western States Petroleum Association dropped $33 million to kill that campaign. It was the largest campaign in Washington State history. We came back and passed the Climate Commitment Act in the Washington State legislature, hard-wiring in 10% of all carbon revenues directly dedicated to tribal nations. And that was our vision for imagining public dollars brought into a mix that could then be leveraged to attract private sector investment, to de-risk that investment and philanthropic investment as well. And so four years in, we've been able to drive $1 billion into the Washington State treasury for local communities and indigenous nations. And so that's an example where we were not only visible, we led, we joined the Seattle environmental community, we traveled every reservation, tribal nation in the state of Washington, and it was successful. We were visible, we were heard, and people saw that we were indispensable to solving the crisis there in the state of Washington. And what do I see on the horizon for the next six months? I see taking that public sector finance, utilizing that to build a system in which we can create a platform for others to directly invest. I've had conversations with those who have spent 40 years of innovative finance with tribal nations. For example, the Calvert Fund. They took 1% of their community fund to issue community bonds to deploy private capital to build tribal economies inside the United States. That was a $100 million raise. Now if we were to take the Climate Commitment Act funding, leverage that to de-risk private sector investment and create a premium green bond, a deep green bond, and a deep blue bond in which we are able to deploy capital directly in partnership with sovereign tribal nations to attract foreign investment that otherwise would not come to the United States and use metrics that bring to life our living systems, where we do have access to tremendous natural capital throughout indigenous communities. So that's what I see on the horizon for our communities, and it's a bright future and I know that our ancestors foretold of this time and we're about to make it happen. But it comes across a couple of, I think a couple of foundational points. One is that we are seeing successes. We're seeing successes even in the fact that we're having this conversation, Robin. There was not that long ago I recall doing some writing for the World Economic Forum only a number of years ago where it was an unusual voice. It was a voice that they didn't know what to do with. And I'm sort of looking sideways out of view here—they didn't know how to position that voice. But here we are in this conversation. That's something that we should recognize for a moment. But it's also the fact that the real change, the real change of how things are listening, is that in our culture we talk about the fact that country is our teacher. It's not us. We are mere spokespeople for it. We are spokespeople for humanity because we are the oldest humanity. But it's country that does the teaching. That's country that is everything. So what I find the most successful is bringing people into country. And I see that, I see that. I see the world being hungry for that change. Everybody I meet is asking themselves questions. Their political systems are volatile. The economic systems are crumbling. The social systems are uncertain. Everybody has questions. We are a small population, but we have access to the support for that. So my challenge is that the change will come when people invite us into those conversations. And I want to make sure it's not seen as a power grab or a money grab or a diversity and inclusion play. This is a responsibility that we are talking about, that we want to invite the rest of the world into. I'm not here to fight for particular money for particular spaces. I'm here to invite you all into our responsibility. Because it's only that way that we're going to have a shared future together. We may not have any shared future at all if we don't find that story. Jensen Huang: Nvidia's Future, Physical AI, Rise of the Agent, Inference Explosion, AI PR Crisis

All-In Podcast(1.0M) ·66:06 ·206.1K views · 2026-03-19
Jensen Huang (CEO, NVIDIA)
The AI revolution is transitioning from simple generative chat to "agentic" systems that perform actual work, requiring a shift from standalone GPUs to massive, disaggregated "AI Factories.
TL;DR The AI revolution is transitioning from simple generative chat to "agentic" systems that perform actual work, requiring a shift from standalone GPUs to massive, disaggregated "AI Factories." This evolution is expected to drive a 10,000x increase in compute demand while...

TL;DR

The AI revolution is transitioning from simple generative chat to "agentic" systems that perform actual work, requiring a shift from standalone GPUs to massive, disaggregated "AI Factories." This evolution is expected to drive a 10,000x increase in compute demand while fundamentally transforming $50 trillion in physical industries like robotics, biology, and manufacturing.

The Shift to AI Factories and Disaggregated Computing

  • From Chips to Infrastructure: Nvidia is evolving from a GPU provider into an "AI Factory" company.
    • Dynamo OS: Jensen Huang introduced "Dynamo" as the operating system for the modern AI factory, designed to turn data into "intelligence" much like 19th-century factories turned water into electricity.
  • Disaggregated Inference: To solve the most complicated computing problems, Nvidia is disaggregating the processing pipeline.
    • Workloads are spread across heterogeneous hardware, including CPUs, GPUs, BlueField networking processors, and specialized Grock processors.
  • The Economics of Scale: Huang argues that a $50 billion data center is actually cheaper than a $30 billion one when measured by output.
    • Throughput Efficiency: High-end factories provide 10x the throughput, resulting in the lowest cost per token.
    • The "Free Chip" Fallacy: Huang posits that even if a competitor’s chips were free, they are "not cheap enough" if they cannot keep up with the state-of-the-art pace of the Nvidia stack.

Agentic AI and the "Open Claw" Movement

  • The Three Stages of AI: The industry has moved rapidly through three inflection points: Generative (Chat GPT), Reasoning (OpenAI’s o1), and now Agentic (systems that use tools and perform tasks).
  • The Personal AI Computer: Huang highlighted "Open Claw" as a cultural and technical milestone.
    • It functions as an open-source operating system for agents, managing memory, skills, scheduling, and I/O.
    • This allows for a "personal artificial intelligence computer" that runs everywhere, from desktops to the cloud.
  • The Token-Augmented Workforce: Nvidia's 43,000 employees are becoming "bionic" through heavy token consumption.
    • Employee Productivity: Huang expects a $500,000-a-year engineer to consume at least $250,000 in tokens annually.
    • Creative Unlock: By automating heavy lifting, engineers are reduced to pure creativity, managing teams of "hundreds of agents" rather than writing lines of code.

Future Verticals: Robotics, Biology, and Space

  • Physical AI: Huang identifies this as the first technology to address the $50 trillion physical industry.
    • Robotics Timeline: High-functioning humanoid robots are expected to transition into reasonable commercial products within the next 3 to 5 years.
    • Supply Chain: While the US leads in software/brains, China remains a "formidable" leader in the micro-electronics and motors necessary for robotics.
  • Digital Biology: Biology is nearing its "ChatGPT moment."
    • Within 5 years, AI will move from understanding chemicals to representing and predicting the dynamics of genes, proteins, and cells.
  • Data Centers in Space: Nvidia is already radiation-hardening its technology for space.
    • Edge Processing: The goal is to perform image processing directly on satellites rather than sending massive data sets back to Earth.
    • Challenge: Cooling remains the primary hurdle in space, as heat can only be managed through radiation rather than conduction or convection.

Geopolitics and National Strategy

  • Market Dynamics in China: Nvidia has dropped from a 95% market share to 0% in China's second-largest market due to export rules.
    • Huang is working with Secretary Lutnick to regain licenses to ship the "American tech stack" globally.
  • National Security Concerns: Huang warns against "AI dumerism" and excessive regulation.
    • The greatest risk is not AI consciousness, but the "diffusion" of AI technology being faster in other countries than in the United States.
  • Supply Chain Resilience: To protect against global conflicts, Nvidia is helping re-industrialize the U.S. (Arizona and Texas) while diversifying manufacturing into Japan, South Korea, and Europe.

Notable Quotes

"The difference between that GPU being 1x price or half-x price is not between $50 billion and $30 billion. Even when the chips are free, it's not cheap enough if you can't keep up with the pace of the technology." - on the Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) for AI factories.
"People pay for information, but people mostly pay for work. Agentic systems get work done." - on the Shift from Chatbots to Agents.
"If that $500,000 engineer did not consume at least $250,000 worth of tokens, I am going to be deeply alarmed. Everything that's too big, too heavy, or takes too long—those ideas are all gone." - on Future Workforce Productivity.
"I still believe that deep science, deep math, and language skills are the ultimate program. As it turns out, the English major could be the most successful computer programmer." - on Advice for Students.
